href="/wiki/List_of_Commando_raids_on_the_Atlantic_Wall" title="List of Commando raids on the Atlantic Wall">Raids on the Atlantic Wall</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Atlantic" title="Battle of the Atlantic">Battle of Atlantic</a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div>\n<p>The <b>Battle of Britain</b> (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <i lang="de">Luftschlacht um England</i>, literally "The Air Battle for England") was a <a href="/wiki/Military_campaign" title="Military campaign">military campaign</a> of the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a>, in which the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> (RAF) defended the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> (UK) against large-scale attacks by <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>\'s air force, the <a href="/wiki/Luftwaffe" title="Luftwaffe">Luftwaffe</a>. It has been described as the first major military campaign fought entirely by air forces.<sup id="cite_ref-Geoffrey_Wellum_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geoffrey_Wellum-25">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>  The British officially recognise the battle\'s duration as being from 10 July until 31 October 1940, which overlaps the period of large-scale night attacks known as <a href="/wiki/The_Blitz" title="The Blitz">the Blitz</a>, that lasted from 7 September 1940 to 11 May 1941.<sup id="cite_ref-RAF_Museum_phases_BoB_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAF_Museum_phases_BoB-26">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\nGerman historians do not accept this subdivision and regard the battle as a single campaign lasting from July 1940 to June 1941, including the Blitz.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201373\xe2\x80\x9374_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201373\xe2\x80\x9374-27">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The primary objective of the German forces was to compel Britain to agree to a negotiated peace settlement. In July 1940 the air and sea blockade began, with the Luftwaffe mainly targeting coastal-shipping convoys, ports and shipping centres, such as <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth" title="Portsmouth">Portsmouth</a>. On 1 August, the Luftwaffe was directed to achieve <a href="/wiki/Air_superiority" class="mw-redirect" title="Air superiority">air superiority</a> over the RAF with the aim of incapacitating <a href="/wiki/RAF_Fighter_Command" title="RAF Fighter Command">RAF Fighter Command</a>; <a href="/wiki/Adlertag" title="Adlertag">12 days later</a>, it shifted the attacks to RAF <a href="/wiki/Airfield" class="mw-redirect" title="Airfield">airfields</a> and <a href="/wiki/Military_infrastructure" class="mw-redirect" title="Military infrastructure">infrastructure</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBungay200031\xe2\x80\x9333_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBungay200031\xe2\x80\x9333-28">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> As the battle progressed, the Luftwaffe also targeted factories involved in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II_aircraft_production" title="World War II aircraft production">aircraft production</a> and strategic infrastructure. Eventually it employed <a href="/wiki/Terror_bombing" class="mw-redirect" title="Terror bombing">terror bombing</a> on areas of political significance and on civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;nb 9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Germans had rapidly overwhelmed France and the <a href="/wiki/Low_Countries" title="Low Countries">Low Countries</a>, leaving Britain to face the threat of invasion by sea. The German high command knew the difficulties of a seaborne attack<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> and its impracticality while the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> controlled the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a> and the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBungay2000_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBungay2000-32">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (July 2018)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> On 16 July, <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> ordered the preparation of <a href="/wiki/Operation_Sea_Lion" title="Operation Sea Lion">Operation Sea Lion</a> as a potential <a href="/wiki/Amphibious_assault" class="mw-redirect" title="Amphibious assault">amphibious</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fallschirmj%C3%A4ger_(Nazi_Germany)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fallschirmj\xc3\xa4ger (Nazi Germany)">airborne</a> assault on Britain, to follow once the Luftwaffe had air superiority over the UK. In September, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Bomber_Command" title="RAF Bomber Command">RAF Bomber Command</a> night raids disrupted the German preparation of converted barges, and the Luftwaffe\'s failure to overwhelm the RAF forced Hitler to postpone and eventually cancel Operation Sea Lion. Germany proved unable to sustain daylight raids, but their continued night-bombing operations on Britain became known as <a href="/wiki/The_Blitz" title="The Blitz">the Blitz</a>. \n</p><p>Historian <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Bungay" title="Stephen Bungay">Stephen Bungay</a> cited Germany\'s failure to destroy Britain\'s <a href="/wiki/Air_defence" class="mw-redirect" title="Air defence">air defences</a> to force an <a href="/wiki/Armistice" title="Armistice">armistice</a> (or even outright surrender) as the first major German defeat in World War II and a crucial turning point in the conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_2000,_p._388_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_2000,_p._388-33">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The Battle of Britain takes its name from <a href="/wiki/This_was_their_finest_hour" title="This was their finest hour">a speech</a> given by Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> to the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a> on 18 June: "What <a href="/wiki/Maxime_Weygand" title="Maxime Weygand">General Weygand</a> called the \'<a href="/wiki/Battle_of_France" title="Battle of France">Battle of France</a>\' is over. I expect that the battle of Britain is about to begin."<sup id="cite_ref-Stacey_1955,_p.18_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stacey_1955,_p.18-34">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toctext">Squadron- and higher-level deployment</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-31"><a href="#Bomber_and_Coastal_Command_contributions"><span class="tocnumber">4.5</span> <span class="toctext">Bomber and Coastal Command contributions</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#Phases_of_the_battle"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Phases of the battle</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-33"><a href="#Small_scale_raids"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Small scale raids</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-34"><a href="#Channel_battles"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Channel battles</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-35"><a href="#Main_assault"><span class="tocnumber">5.3</span> <span class="toctext">Main assault</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-36"><a href="#Assault_on_RAF:_radar_and_airfields"><span class="tocnumber">5.3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Assault on RAF: radar and airfields</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-37"><a href="#Raids_on_British_cities"><span class="tocnumber">5.3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Raids on British cities</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-38"><a href="#Attacks_on_airfields_from_24_August"><span class="tocnumber">5.3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Attacks on airfields from 24 August</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-39"><a href="#Assessment_of_attempt_to_destroy_the_RAF"><span class="tocnumber">5.3.4</span> <span class="toctext">Assessment of attempt to destroy the RAF</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-40"><a href="#Day_and_night_attacks_on_London:_start_of_the_Blitz"><span class="tocnumber">5.4</span> <span class="toctext">Day and night attacks on London: start of the Blitz</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-41"><a href="#Night_time_Blitz,_fighter-bomber_day_raids"><span class="tocnumber">5.5</span> <span class="toctext">Night time Blitz, fighter-bomber day raids</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-42"><a href="#Royal_family"><span class="tocnumber">5.5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Royal family</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-43"><a href="#Attrition_statistics"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Attrition statistics</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-44"><a href="#Propaganda"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Propaganda</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-45"><a href="#Aftermath"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Aftermath</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-46"><a href="#Battle_of_Britain_Day"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Battle of Britain Day</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-47"><a href="#Memorials_and_museums"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Memorials and museums</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-48"><a href="#In_popular_culture"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">In popular culture</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-49"><a href="#Media_adaptations"><span class="tocnumber">11.1</span> <span class="toctext">Media adaptations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-50"><a href="#In_books"><span class="tocnumber">11.2</span> <span class="toctext">In books</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-51"><a href="#Miscellaneous"><span class="tocnumber">11.3</span> <span class="toctext">Miscellaneous</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-52"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-53"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-54"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-55"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-56"><a href="#General"><span class="tocnumber">15.1</span> <span class="toctext">General</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-57"><a href="#Autobiographies_and_biographies"><span class="tocnumber">15.2</span> <span class="toctext">Autobiographies and biographies</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-58"><a href="#Aircraft"><span class="tocnumber">15.3</span> <span class="toctext">Aircraft</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-59"><a href="#Additional_references"><span class="tocnumber">15.4</span> <span class="toctext">Additional references</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Strategic_bombing_during_World_War_I" title="Strategic bombing during World War I">Strategic bombing during World War I</a> introduced air attacks intended to panic civilian targets and led in 1918 to the amalgamation of British army and navy air services into the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> (RAF).<sup id="cite_ref-Luftwaffe_Origins_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Luftwaffe_Origins-35">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Its first <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_the_Air_Staff_(United_Kingdom)" title="Chief of the Air Staff (United Kingdom)">Chief of the Air Staff</a> <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Trenchard,_1st_Viscount_Trenchard" title="Hugh Trenchard, 1st Viscount Trenchard">Hugh Trenchard</a> was among the military strategists in the 1920s like <a href="/wiki/Giulio_Douhet" title="Giulio Douhet">Giulio Douhet</a> who saw air warfare as a new way to overcome the stalemate of <a href="/wiki/Trench_warfare" title="Trench warfare">trench warfare</a>. Interception was nearly impossible with fighter planes no faster than bombers. Their view (expressed vividly in 1932) was that <a href="/wiki/The_bomber_will_always_get_through" title="The bomber will always get through">the bomber will always get through</a>, and the only defence was a deterrent bomber force capable of matching retaliation. Predictions were made that a bomber offensive would quickly cause thousands of deaths and civilian hysteria leading to capitulation, but widespread pacifism contributed to a reluctance to provide resources.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBishop201014\xe2\x80\x9318_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBishop201014\xe2\x80\x9318-36">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Developing_air_strategies">Developing air strategies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Developing air strategies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Germany was forbidden to have a military air force by the 1919 <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a>, but developed air crew training in civilian and <a href="/wiki/Gliding" title="Gliding">sport flying</a>. Following a 1923 memorandum, the <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Luft_Hansa" title="Deutsche Luft Hansa">Deutsche Luft Hansa</a> airline developed designs for aircraft which could carry passengers and freight, and also be readily adapted into bombers, including the <a href="/wiki/Junkers_Ju_52" title="Junkers Ju 52">Junkers Ju 52</a>. In 1926 the secret <a href="/wiki/Lipetsk_fighter-pilot_school" title="Lipetsk fighter-pilot school">Lipetsk fighter-pilot school</a> began operating.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBishop201018,_24\xe2\x80\x9326_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBishop201018,_24\xe2\x80\x9326-37">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Erhard_Milch" title="Erhard Milch">Erhard Milch</a> organised rapid expansion, and following the 1933 <a href="/wiki/Nazi_seizure_of_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Nazi seizure of power">Nazi seizure of power</a> his subordinate Robert Knauss formulated a <a href="/wiki/Deterrence_theory" title="Deterrence theory">deterrence theory</a> incorporating <a href="/wiki/Giulio_Douhet" title="Giulio Douhet">Douhet\'s</a> ideas and <a href="/wiki/Alfred_von_Tirpitz#Second_Naval_Bill" title="Alfred von Tirpitz">Tirpitz\'s "risk theory"</a>, which proposed a fleet of <a href="/wiki/Heavy_bomber" title="Heavy bomber">heavy bombers</a> to deter a preventive attack by France and Poland before Germany could fully rearm.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray2002&#91;httpwwwibiblioorghyperwarAAFAAF-LuftwaffeAAF-Luftwaffe-1htmlcn32_6\xe2\x80\x937&#93;_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray2002[httpwwwibiblioorghyperwarAAFAAF-LuftwaffeAAF-Luftwaffe-1htmlcn32_6\xe2\x80\x937]-38">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> A 1933\xe2\x80\x9334 <a href="/wiki/Military_simulation" title="Military simulation">war game</a> indicated a need for fighters and anti-aircraft protection as well as bombers. On 1 March 1935 the <a href="/wiki/Luftwaffe" title="Luftwaffe">Luftwaffe</a> was formally announced, with <a href="/wiki/Walther_Wever_(general)" title="Walther Wever (general)">Walther Wever</a> as Chief of Staff. The 1935 Luftwaffe doctrine for "Conduct of the Air War" (<i>Die Luftkriegf\xc3\xbchrung)</i> set air power within the overall military strategy, with critical tasks of attaining (local and temporary) <a href="/wiki/Air_supremacy" title="Air supremacy">air superiority</a> and providing battlefield support for army and naval forces. <a href="/wiki/Strategic_bombing" title="Strategic bombing">Strategic bombing</a> of industries and transport could be decisive longer term options, dependent on opportunity or preparations by the army and navy, to overcome a stalemate or used when only destruction of the enemy\'s economy would be conclusive.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray2002&#91;httpwwwibiblioorghyperwarAAFAAF-LuftwaffeAAF-Luftwaffe-1htmlcn36_7\xe2\x80\x939&#93;_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray2002[httpwwwibiblioorghyperwarAAFAAF-LuftwaffeAAF-Luftwaffe-1htmlcn36_7\xe2\x80\x939]-39">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBungay200036\xe2\x80\x9339_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBungay200036\xe2\x80\x9339-40">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The list excluded bombing civilians to destroy homes or undermine morale, as that was considered a waste of strategic effort, but the doctrine allowed revenge attacks if German civilians were bombed. A revised edition was issued in 1940, and the continuing central principle of Luftwaffe doctrine was that destruction of enemy armed forces was of primary importance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201342\xe2\x80\x9343_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201342\xe2\x80\x9343-41">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The RAF responded to Luftwaffe developments with its 1934 Expansion Plan A rearmament scheme, and in 1936 it was restructured into <a href="/wiki/RAF_Bomber_Command" title="RAF Bomber Command">Bomber Command</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Coastal_Command" title="RAF Coastal Command">Coastal Command</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Training_Command" title="RAF Training Command">Training Command</a> and <a href="/wiki/RAF_Fighter_Command" title="RAF Fighter Command">Fighter Command</a>. The latter was under <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Dowding,_1st_Baron_Dowding" class="mw-redirect" title="Hugh Dowding, 1st Baron Dowding">Hugh Dowding</a>, who opposed the doctrine that bombers were unstoppable: the invention of radar at that time could allow early detection, and prototype monoplane fighters were significantly faster. Priorities were disputed, but in December 1937 the Minister in charge of defence coordination Sir <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Inskip,_1st_Viscount_Caldecote" title="Thomas Inskip, 1st Viscount Caldecote">Thomas Inskip</a> decided in Dowding\'s favour, that "The role of our air force is not an early knock-out blow" but rather was "to prevent the Germans from knocking us out" and fighter squadrons were just as necessary as bomber squadrons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBishop201018\xe2\x80\x9324_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBishop201018\xe2\x80\x9324-42">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeighton199612\xe2\x80\x9313_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeighton199612\xe2\x80\x9313-43">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a>, the Luftwaffe in the <a href="/wiki/Condor_Legion" title="Condor Legion">Condor Legion</a> tried out air fighting tactics and their new aeroplanes. <a href="/wiki/Wolfram_Freiherr_von_Richthofen" title="Wolfram Freiherr von Richthofen">Wolfram von Richthofen</a> became an exponent of air power providing ground support to other services.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBishop201026_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBishop201026-44">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The difficulty of accurately hitting targets prompted <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Udet" title="Ernst Udet">Ernst Udet</a> to require that all new bombers had to be <a href="/wiki/Dive_bomber" title="Dive bomber">dive bombers</a>, and led to the development of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Beams" title="Battle of the Beams">Knickebein</a> system for night time navigation. Priority was given to producing large numbers of smaller aeroplanes, and plans for <a href="/wiki/Ural_bomber" title="Ural bomber">a long range four engined strategic bomber</a> were delayed.<sup id="cite_ref-Luftwaffe_Origins_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Luftwaffe_Origins-35">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBungay200039\xe2\x80\x9340_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBungay200039\xe2\x80\x9340-45">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_stages_of_World_War_II">First stages of World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: First stages of World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Winston_Churchill_-_19086236948.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Sir_Winston_Churchill_-_19086236948.jpg/170px-Sir_Winston_Churchill_-_19086236948.jpg" width="170" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Sir_Winston_Churchill_-_19086236948.jpg/255px-Sir_Winston_Churchill_-_19086236948.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Sir_Winston_Churchill_-_19086236948.jpg/340px-Sir_Winston_Churchill_-_19086236948.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3052" data-file-height="3884" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Winston_Churchill_-_19086236948.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>, British <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister</a>, in 1941</div></div></div><p> The early stages of World War II saw successful German invasions on the continent aided decisively by the air power of the Luftwaffe, which was able to establish tactical air superiority with great efficiency. The speed with which German forces defeated most of the defending armies in <a href="/wiki/German_occupation_of_Norway" title="German occupation of Norway">Norway</a> in early 1940 created a significant political crisis in Britain. In early May 1940, the <a href="/wiki/Norway_Debate" title="Norway Debate">Norway Debate</a> questioned the fitness for office of the British <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Neville_Chamberlain" title="Neville Chamberlain">Neville Chamberlain</a>. On 10 May, the same day <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> became British Prime Minister, the Germans initiated the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_France" title="Battle of France">Battle of France</a> with an aggressive invasion of French territory. <a href="/wiki/RAF_Fighter_Command" title="RAF Fighter Command">RAF Fighter Command</a> was desperately short of trained pilots and aircraft, but despite the objections of its commander <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Dowding,_1st_Baron_Dowding" class="mw-redirect" title="Hugh Dowding, 1st Baron Dowding">Hugh Dowding</a> that the diversion of his forces would leave home defences under-strength, Churchill sent fighter squadrons, the <a href="/wiki/British_Air_Forces_in_France" title="British Air Forces in France">Air Component</a> of the <a href="/wiki/British_Expeditionary_Force_(World_War_II)" title="British Expeditionary Force (World War II)">British Expeditionary Force</a>, to support operations in France,<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> where the RAF suffered heavy losses.<sup id="cite_ref-short_hist_99_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-short_hist_99-47">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/Dunkirk_evacuation" title="Dunkirk evacuation">evacuation of British and French soldiers from Dunkirk</a> and the French surrender on 22 June 1940, Hitler mainly focused his energies on the possibility of invading the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> in the belief that the British, defeated on the continent and without European allies, would quickly come to terms.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The Germans were so convinced of an imminent armistice that they began constructing street decorations for the homecoming parades of victorious troops.<sup id="cite_ref-smith1942_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smith1942-50">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Although the British <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Affairs" title="Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs">Foreign Secretary</a>, <a href="/wiki/E._F._L._Wood,_1st_Earl_of_Halifax" class="mw-redirect" title="E. F. L. Wood, 1st Earl of Halifax">Lord Halifax</a>, and certain elements of the British public favoured a negotiated peace with an ascendant Germany, Churchill and a majority of his Cabinet refused to consider an armistice.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Instead, Churchill used his skilful rhetoric to harden public opinion against capitulation and to prepare the British for a long war.\n</p><p>The Battle of Britain has the unusual distinction that it gained its name before being fought. The name is derived from the <i><a href="/wiki/This_was_their_finest_hour" title="This was their finest hour">This was their finest hour</a></i> speech delivered by Winston Churchill in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a> on 18 June, more than three weeks prior to the generally accepted date for the start of the battle:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>... What <a href="/wiki/Maxime_Weygand" title="Maxime Weygand">General Weygand</a> has called The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_France" title="Battle of France">Battle of France</a> is over. The battle of Britain is about to begin. Upon this battle depends the survival of <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Christian civilisation</a>. Upon it depends our own British life and the long continuity of our institutions and our <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">Empire</a>. The whole fury and might of the enemy must very soon be turned on us. Hitler knows that he will have to break us in this island or lose the war. If we can stand up to him, all Europe may be free and the life of the world may move forward into broad, sunlit uplands. But if we fail, then the whole world, including the United States, including all that we have known and cared for, will sink into the abyss of a new <a href="/wiki/Dark_Ages_(historiography)" title="Dark Ages (historiography)">Dark Age</a> made more sinister, and perhaps more protracted, by the lights of a perverted science. Let us therefore brace ourselves to our duties, and so bear ourselves that, if the British Empire and its Commonwealth last for a thousand years, men will still say, "This was their finest hour".<sup id="cite_ref-Stacey_1955,_p.18_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stacey_1955,_p.18-34">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Winston Churchill</cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="German_aims_and_directives">German aims and directives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: German aims and directives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Adolf_Hitler_Berghof-1936.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Adolf_Hitler_Berghof-1936.jpg/170px-Adolf_Hitler_Berghof-1936.jpg" width="170" height="246" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ee/Adolf_Hitler_Berghof-1936.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="227" data-file-height="328" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Adolf_Hitler_Berghof-1936.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_Germany" title="Chancellor of Germany">Chancellor</a> of Germany, in 1933</div></div></div><p> From the outset of his rise to power, Hitler expressed admiration for Britain, and throughout the Battle period he sought neutrality or a peace treaty with Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBungay200027\xe2\x80\x9331_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBungay200027\xe2\x80\x9331-54">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> In a secret conference on 23 May 1939, Hitler set out his rather contradictory strategy that an attack on Poland was essential and "will only be successful if the Western Powers keep out of it. If this is impossible, then it will be better to attack in the West and to settle Poland at the same time" with a surprise attack. "If Holland and Belgium are successfully occupied and held, and if France is also defeated, the fundamental conditions for a successful war against England will have been secured. England can then be blockaded from Western France at close quarters by the Air Force, while the Navy with its submarines extend the range of the blockade."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1964589\xe2\x80\x93593_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1964589\xe2\x80\x93593-55">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>When war commenced, Hitler and the OKW (<i><a href="/wiki/Oberkommando_der_Wehrmacht" title="Oberkommando der Wehrmacht">Oberkommando der Wehrmacht</a></i> or "High Command of the Armed Forces") issued a <a href="/wiki/List_of_Adolf_Hitler%27s_directives" title="List of Adolf Hitler&#39;s directives">series of Directives</a> ordering planning and stating strategic objectives. "Directive No. 1 for the Conduct of the War" dated 31 August 1939 instructed the <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Poland" title="Invasion of Poland">invasion of Poland</a> on 1 September as <a href="/wiki/Fall_Weiss_(1939)" title="Fall Weiss (1939)">planned</a>. Potentially, Luftwaffe "operations against England" were to "dislocate English imports, the armaments industry, and the transport of troops to France. Any favourable opportunity of an effective attack on concentrated units of the English Navy, particularly on battleships or aircraft carriers, will be exploited. The decision regarding attacks on London is reserved to me. Attacks on the English homeland are to be prepared, bearing in mind that inconclusive results with insufficient forces are to be avoided in all circumstances."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1964712\xe2\x80\x93713_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1964712\xe2\x80\x93713-57">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-F\xc3\xbchrer_Directive_1_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-F\xc3\xbchrer_Directive_1-58">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Both France and the UK declared war on Germany; on 9 October Hitler\'s "Directive No. 6" planned the offensive to defeat these allies and "win as much territory as possible in the Netherlands, Belgium, and northern France to serve as a base for the successful prosecution of the air and sea war against England".<sup id="cite_ref-Directive_6_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Directive_6-59">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> On 29 November OKW "Directive No. 9 \xe2\x80\x93 Instructions For Warfare Against The Economy Of The Enemy" stated that once this coastline had been secured, the Luftwaffe together with the <a href="/wiki/Kriegsmarine" title="Kriegsmarine">Kriegsmarine</a> (German Navy) was to blockade UK ports with sea mines, attack shipping and warships, and make air attacks on shore installations and industrial production. This directive remained in force in the first phase of the Battle of Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-Directive_9_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Directive_9-60">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Murray_33_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murray_33-61">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> It was reinforced on 24 May during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_France" title="Battle of France">Battle of France</a> by "Directive No. 13" which authorised the Luftwaffe "to attack the English homeland in the fullest manner, as soon as sufficient forces are available. This attack will be opened by an annihilating reprisal for English attacks on the Ruhr Basin."<sup id="cite_ref-Magenheimer_24_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magenheimer_24-62">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the end of June 1940, Germany had defeated Britain\'s allies on the continent, and on 30 June the OKW Chief of Staff <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Jodl" title="Alfred Jodl">Alfred Jodl</a> issued his review of options to increase pressure on Britain to agree to a negotiated peace. The first priority was to eliminate the RAF and gain <a href="/wiki/Air_supremacy" title="Air supremacy">air supremacy</a>. Intensified air attacks against shipping and the economy could affect food supplies and civilian morale in the long term. Reprisal attacks of terror bombing had the potential to cause quicker capitulation, but the effect on morale was uncertain. Once the Luftwaffe had control of the air, and the UK economy had been weakened, an invasion would be a last resort or a final strike ("<i>Todesstoss</i>") after Britain had already been conquered, but could have a quick result.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (February 2018)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> On the same day, the Luftwaffe Commander-in-Chief <a href="/wiki/Hermann_G%C3%B6ring" title="Hermann G\xc3\xb6ring">Hermann G\xc3\xb6ring</a> issued his operational directive; to destroy the RAF, thus protecting German industry, and also to block overseas supplies to Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBungay200031\xe2\x80\x9333,_122_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBungay200031\xe2\x80\x9333,_122-63">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Murray_44-5_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murray_44-5-64">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The German Supreme Command argued over the practicality of these options.\n</p><p>In "Directive No. 16 \xe2\x80\x93 On preparations for a landing operation against England" on 16 July,<sup id="cite_ref-F\xc3\xbchrer_Directive_16_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-F\xc3\xbchrer_Directive_16-65">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Hitler required readiness by mid-August for the possibility of an invasion he called <a href="/wiki/Operation_Sea_Lion" title="Operation Sea Lion">Operation Sea Lion</a>, unless the British agreed to negotiations. The Luftwaffe reported that it would be ready to launch its major attack early in August. The Kriegsmarine Commander-in-Chief, Grand Admiral <a href="/wiki/Erich_Raeder" title="Erich Raeder">Erich Raeder</a>, continued to highlight the impracticality of these plans, and said sea invasion could not take place before early 1941. Hitler now argued that Britain was holding out in hope of assistance from Russia, and the <a href="/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa" title="Operation Barbarossa">Soviet Union was to be invaded</a> by mid 1941.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBungay2000110\xe2\x80\x93114_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBungay2000110\xe2\x80\x93114-66">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> G\xc3\xb6ring met his air fleet commanders, and on 24 July issued "Tasks and Goals" of firstly gaining <a href="/wiki/Air_supremacy" title="Air supremacy">air supremacy</a>, secondly protecting invasion forces and attacking the Royal Navy\'s ships. Thirdly, they were to blockade imports, bombing harbours and stores of supplies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201372_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201372-67">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Hitler\'s "Directive No. 17 \xe2\x80\x93 For the conduct of air and sea warfare against England" issued on 1 August attempted to keep all the options open. The Luftwaffe\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Adlertag" title="Adlertag">Adlertag</a></i> campaign was to start around 5 August, subject to weather, with the aim of gaining air superiority over southern England as a necessary precondition of invasion, to give credibility to the threat and give Hitler the option of ordering the invasion. The intention was to incapacitate the RAF so much that the UK would feel open to air attack, and would begin peace negotiations. It was also to isolate the UK and damage war production, beginning an effective blockade.<sup id="cite_ref-Dir_17_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dir_17-68">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Following severe Luftwaffe losses, Hitler agreed at a 14 September OKW conference that the air campaign was to intensify regardless of invasion plans. On 16 September, G\xc3\xb6ring gave the order for this change in strategy,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery200187\xe2\x80\x9389_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery200187\xe2\x80\x9389-69">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> to the first independent <a href="/wiki/Strategic_bombing" title="Strategic bombing">strategic bombing</a> campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201390_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201390-70">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Negotiated_peace_or_neutrality">Negotiated peace or neutrality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Negotiated peace or neutrality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Adolf Hitler\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Mein_Kampf" title="Mein Kampf">Mein Kampf</a></i> of 1923 mostly set out his hatreds: he only admired ordinary German <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> soldiers and Britain, which he saw as an ally against communism. In 1935 Hermann G\xc3\xb6ring welcomed news that Britain as a potential ally was rearming. In 1936 he promised assistance to defend the British Empire, asking only a free hand in Eastern Europe, and repeated this to <a href="/wiki/Edward_Wood,_1st_Earl_of_Halifax" title="Edward Wood, 1st Earl of Halifax">Lord Halifax</a> in 1937. That year, <a href="/wiki/Joachim_von_Ribbentrop" title="Joachim von Ribbentrop">von Ribbentrop</a> met Churchill with a similar proposal; when rebuffed, he told Churchill that interference with German domination would mean war. To Hitler\'s great annoyance, all his diplomacy failed to stop Britain from declaring war when he invaded Poland. During the fall of France, he repeatedly discussed peace efforts with his generals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBungay200027\xe2\x80\x9331_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBungay200027\xe2\x80\x9331-54">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>When Churchill came to power, there was still wide support for Halifax, who as <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Foreign_and_Commonwealth_Affairs" title="Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs">Foreign Secretary</a> openly argued for peace negotiations in the tradition of British diplomacy, to secure British independence without war. On 20 May, Halifax secretly requested a Swedish businessman to make contact with G\xc3\xb6ring to open negotiations. Shortly afterwards, in the <a href="/wiki/May_1940_War_Cabinet_Crisis" title="May 1940 War Cabinet Crisis">May 1940 War Cabinet Crisis</a>, Halifax argued for negotiations involving the Italians, but this was rejected by Churchill with majority support. An approach made through the Swedish ambassador on 22 June was reported to Hitler, making peace negotiations seem feasible. Throughout July, as the battle started, the Germans made wider attempts to find a diplomatic solution.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBungay20009\xe2\x80\x9313,_33_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBungay20009\xe2\x80\x9313,_33-71">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> On 2 July, the day the armed forces were asked to start preliminary planning for an invasion, Hitler got von Ribbentrop to draft a speech offering peace negotiations. On 19 July Hitler made this speech to the German Parliament in Berlin, appealing "to reason and common sense", and said he could "see no reason why this war should go on".<sup id="cite_ref-My_Last_Appeal_to_Great_Britain_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-My_Last_Appeal_to_Great_Britain-72">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> His sombre conclusion was received in silence, but he did not suggest negotiations and this was effectively an ultimatum which was rejected by the British government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBishop2010114\xe2\x80\x93115_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBishop2010114\xe2\x80\x93115-73">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201368\xe2\x80\x9369_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201368\xe2\x80\x9369-74">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Halifax kept trying to arrange peace until he was sent to Washington in December as ambassador,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBungay200013_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBungay200013-75">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> and in January 1941 Hitler expressed continued interest in negotiating peace with Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery2001109_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery2001109-76">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Blockade_and_siege">Blockade and siege</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Blockade and siege">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>A May 1939 planning exercise by <a href="/wiki/Luftflotte_3" title="Luftflotte 3">Luftflotte 3</a> found that the Luftwaffe lacked the means to do much damage to Britain\'s war economy beyond laying <a href="/wiki/Naval_mine" title="Naval mine">naval mines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBungay200032_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBungay200032-77">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\nThe Head of Luftwaffe intelligence <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Schmid" title="Joseph Schmid">Joseph "Beppo" Schmid</a> presented a report on 22 November 1939, stating that "Of all Germany\'s possible enemies, Britain is the most dangerous."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBungay2000ii_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBungay2000ii-78">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> This "Proposal for the Conduct of Air Warfare" argued for a <a href="/wiki/Blockade_of_Germany_(1939%E2%80%9345)" class="mw-redirect" title="Blockade of Germany (1939\xe2\x80\x9345)">counter to the British blockade</a> and said "Key is to paralyse the British trade".<sup id="cite_ref-Directive_9_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Directive_9-60">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Instead of the Wehrmacht attacking the French, the Luftwaffe <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Atlantic" title="Battle of the Atlantic">with naval assistance</a> was to block imports to Britain and attack seaports. "Should the enemy resort to terror measures\xe2\x80\x94for example, to attack our towns in western Germany" they could retaliate by bombing industrial centres and London. Parts of this appeared on 29 November in "Directive No. 9" as future actions once the coast had been conquered.<sup id="cite_ref-Murray_33_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murray_33-61">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> On 24 May 1940 "Directive No. 13" authorised attacks on the blockade targets, as well as retaliation for RAF bombing of industrial targets in the Ruhr.<sup id="cite_ref-Magenheimer_24_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magenheimer_24-62">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the defeat of France the OKW felt they had won the war, and some more pressure would persuade Britain. On 30 June the OKW Chief of Staff <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Jodl" title="Alfred Jodl">Alfred Jodl</a> issued his paper setting out options: the first was to increase attacks on shipping, economic targets and the RAF: air attacks and food shortages were expected to break morale and lead to capitulation. Destruction of the RAF was the first priority, and invasion would be a last resort. G\xc3\xb6ring\'s operational directive issued the same day ordered destruction of the RAF to clear the way for attacks cutting off seaborne supplies to Britain. It made no mention of invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-Murray_44-5_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murray_44-5-64">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBungay200031,_110,_122_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBungay200031,_110,_122-79">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Invasion_plans">Invasion plans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Invasion plans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Operation_Sea_Lion" title="Operation Sea Lion">Operation Sea Lion</a></div>\n<p>In November 1939, the OKW reviewed the potential for an air- and seaborne invasion of Britain: the <a href="/wiki/Kriegsmarine" title="Kriegsmarine">Kriegsmarine</a> (German Navy) was faced with the threat the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a>\'s larger <a href="/wiki/Home_Fleet" title="Home Fleet">Home Fleet</a> posed to a crossing of the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a>, and together with the <a href="/wiki/German_Army_(Wehrmacht)" title="German Army (Wehrmacht)">German Army</a> viewed <a href="/wiki/Air_supremacy" title="Air supremacy">control of airspace</a> as a necessary precondition. The German navy thought air superiority alone was insufficient; the German naval staff had already produced a study (in 1939) on the possibility of an invasion of Britain and concluded that it also required naval superiority.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The Luftwaffe said invasion could only be "the final act in an already victorious war."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBishop2010106\xe2\x80\x93107_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBishop2010106\xe2\x80\x93107-81">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Hitler first discussed the idea of an invasion at a 21 May 1940 meeting with Grand Admiral Erich Raeder, who stressed the difficulties and his own preference for a blockade. OKW Chief of Staff Jodl\'s 30 June report described invasion as a last resort once the British economy had been damaged and the Luftwaffe had full air superiority. On 2 July, OKW requested preliminary plans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBungay200031\xe2\x80\x9333_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBungay200031\xe2\x80\x9333-28">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201368\xe2\x80\x9369_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201368\xe2\x80\x9369-74">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\nIn Britain, Churchill described "the great invasion scare" as "serving a very useful purpose" by "keeping every man and woman tuned to a high pitch of readiness".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBishop201070\xe2\x80\x9371_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBishop201070\xe2\x80\x9371-82">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> On 10 July, he advised the War Cabinet that invasion could be ignored, as it "would be a most hazardous and suicidal operation".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeighton199651_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeighton199651-83">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 11 July, Hitler agreed with Raeder that invasion would be a last resort, and the Luftwaffe advised that gaining air superiority would take 14 to 28 days. Hitler met his army chiefs, <a href="/wiki/Walther_von_Brauchitsch" title="Walther von Brauchitsch">von Brauchitsch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Franz_Halder" title="Franz Halder">Halder</a>, who presented detailed plans on the assumption that the navy would provide safe transport.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBungay2000111_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBungay2000111-84">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Hitler showed no interest in the details, but on 16 July he issued Directive No. 16 ordering preparations for <a href="/wiki/Operation_Sea_Lion" title="Operation Sea Lion">Operation Sea Lion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBishop2010107\xe2\x80\x93108_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBishop2010107\xe2\x80\x93108-85">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The navy insisted on a narrow beachhead and an extended period for landing troops; the army rejected these plans: the Luftwaffe could begin an air attack in August. Hitler held a meeting of his army and navy chiefs on 31 July. The navy said 22 September was the earliest possible date, and proposed postponement until the following year, but Hitler preferred September. He then told von Brauchitsch and Halder that he would decide on the landing operation eight to fourteen days after the air attack began. On 1 August he issued Directive No. 17 for intensified air and sea warfare, to begin with <a href="/wiki/Adlertag" title="Adlertag">Adlertag</a> on or after 5 August subject to weather, keeping options open for negotiated peace or blockade and siege.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBungay2000113\xe2\x80\x93114_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBungay2000113\xe2\x80\x93114-86">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Independent_air_attack">Independent air attack</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Independent air attack">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Under the continuing influence of the 1935 "Conduct of the Air War" doctrine, the main focus of the Luftwaffe command (including G\xc3\xb6ring) was in concentrating attacks to destroy enemy armed forces on the battlefield, and "<a href="/wiki/Blitzkrieg" title="Blitzkrieg">blitzkrieg</a>" <a href="/wiki/Close_air_support" title="Close air support">close air support</a> of the army succeeded brilliantly. They reserved <a href="/wiki/Strategic_bombing_during_World_War_II" title="Strategic bombing during World War II">strategic bombing</a> for a stalemate situation or revenge attacks, but doubted if this could be decisive on its own and regarded bombing civilians to destroy homes or undermine morale as a waste of strategic effort.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201342\xe2\x80\x9343,_60\xe2\x80\x9365_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201342\xe2\x80\x9343,_60\xe2\x80\x9365-87">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeinz_Magenheimer2015&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidfsFACgAAQBAJpgPT20_20&#93;_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeinz_Magenheimer2015[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidfsFACgAAQBAJpgPT20_20]-88">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The defeat of France in June 1940 introduced the prospect for the first time of independent air action against Britain. A July <i><a href="/wiki/1st_Air_Corps_(Germany)" title="1st Air Corps (Germany)">Fliegercorps I</a></i> paper asserted that Germany was by definition an air power: "Its chief weapon against England is the Air Force, then the Navy, followed by the landing forces and the Army." In 1940, the Luftwaffe would undertake a "<i>strategic offensive</i> ... on its own and independent of the other services", according to an April 1944 German account of their military mission. G\xc3\xb6ring was convinced that strategic bombing could win objectives which were beyond the army and navy, and gain political advantages in the Third Reich for the Luftwaffe and himself.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201366\xe2\x80\x9367,_70,_75,_690_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201366\xe2\x80\x9367,_70,_75,_690-89">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> He expected air warfare to decisively force Britain to negotiate, as all in the OKW hoped, and the Luftwaffe took little interest to planning to support an invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBungay2000114_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBungay2000114-90">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Murray_44-5_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murray_44-5-64">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Opposing_forces">Opposing forces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Opposing forces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_of_the_Battle_of_Britain" title="Aircraft of the Battle of Britain">Aircraft of the Battle of Britain</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/RAF_Fighter_Command_Order_of_Battle_1940" title="RAF Fighter Command Order of Battle 1940">RAF Fighter Command Order of Battle 1940</a> and <a href="/wiki/Luftwaffe_Order_of_Battle_August_1940" title="Luftwaffe Order of Battle August 1940">Luftwaffe Order of Battle August 1940</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_officially_accredited_Battle_of_Britain_squadrons" class="mw-redirect" title="List of officially accredited Battle of Britain squadrons">List of officially accredited Battle of Britain squadrons</a></div>\n<p>The <i>Luftwaffe</i> faced a more capable opponent than any it had previously met: a sizeable, highly coordinated, well-supplied, modern air force.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fighters">Fighters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Fighters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <i>Luftwaffe\'</i>s <a href="/wiki/Bf_109E" class="mw-redirect" title="Bf 109E">Messerschmitt Bf 109E</a> and <a href="/wiki/Messerschmitt_Bf_110" title="Messerschmitt Bf 110">Bf 110C</a> fought against the RAF\'s workhorse <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Hurricane_variants#Hurricane_Mk_I" title="Hawker Hurricane variants">Hurricane Mk I</a> and the less numerous <a href="/wiki/Supermarine_Spitfire_(early_Merlin_powered_variants)#Mk_I_(Type_300)" class="mw-redirect" title="Supermarine Spitfire (early Merlin powered variants)">Spitfire Mk I</a>; Hurricanes outnumbered Spitfires in RAF Fighter Command by about 2:1 when war broke out.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The Bf 109E had a better climb rate and was up to 40&#160;mph faster in level flight than the Rotol (constant speed propeller) equipped Hurricane Mk I, depending on altitude.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The speed and climb disparity with the original non-Rotol Hurricane was even greater. By mid-1940, all RAF Spitfire and Hurricane fighter squadrons converted to 100 octane aviation fuel,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> which allowed their <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Merlin" title="Rolls-Royce Merlin">Merlin</a> engines to generate significantly more power and an approximately 30&#160;mph increase in speed at low altitudes<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> through the use of an <a href="/wiki/War_emergency_power" title="War emergency power">Emergency Boost Override</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> In September 1940, the more powerful Mk IIa series 1 Hurricanes started entering service in small numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> This version was capable of a maximum speed of 342&#160;mph (550&#160;km/h), some 20&#160;mph more than the original (non-Rotol) Mk I, though it was still 15 to 20&#160;mph slower than a Bf 109 (depending on altitude).<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hawker_Hurricane_R4118_arrives_RIAT_Fairford_10thJuly2014_arp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Hawker_Hurricane_R4118_arrives_RIAT_Fairford_10thJuly2014_arp.jpg/220px-Hawker_Hurricane_R4118_arrives_RIAT_Fairford_10thJuly2014_arp.jpg" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Hawker_Hurricane_R4118_arrives_RIAT_Fairford_10thJuly2014_arp.jpg/330px-Hawker_Hurricane_R4118_arrives_RIAT_Fairford_10thJuly2014_arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Hawker_Hurricane_R4118_arrives_RIAT_Fairford_10thJuly2014_arp.jpg/440px-Hawker_Hurricane_R4118_arrives_RIAT_Fairford_10thJuly2014_arp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1840" data-file-height="1282" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hawker_Hurricane_R4118_arrives_RIAT_Fairford_10thJuly2014_arp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hawker_Hurricane" title="Hawker Hurricane">Hawker Hurricane</a> R4118 fought in the Battle of Britain. Here it arrives at the 2014 <a href="/wiki/Royal_International_Air_Tattoo" title="Royal International Air Tattoo">Royal International Air Tattoo</a>, <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:602sqdn-spit1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/602sqdn-spit1.jpg/220px-602sqdn-spit1.jpg" width="220" height="99" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/602sqdn-spit1.jpg/330px-602sqdn-spit1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/602sqdn-spit1.jpg/440px-602sqdn-spit1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="740" data-file-height="334" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:602sqdn-spit1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>X4382</i>, a late production Spitfire Mk I of <a href="/wiki/No._602_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 602 Squadron RAF">602 Squadron</a> flown by <a href="/wiki/Pilot_Officer" class="mw-redirect" title="Pilot Officer">P/O</a> <a href="/wiki/Osgood_Hanbury" title="Osgood Hanbury">Osgood Hanbury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Westhampnett" title="Westhampnett">Westhampnett</a>, September 1940</div></div></div>\n<p>The performance of the Spitfire over <a href="/wiki/Dunkirk_evacuation" title="Dunkirk evacuation">Dunkirk</a> came as a surprise to the <i><a href="/wiki/Jagdwaffe" title="Jagdwaffe">Jagdwaffe</a></i>, although the German pilots retained a strong belief that the 109 was the superior fighter.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The British fighters were equipped with eight Browning <a href="/wiki/.303_British" title=".303 British">.303</a> (7.7mm) machine guns, while most Bf 109Es had two <a href="/wiki/7.92_x_57mm_Mauser" class="mw-redirect" title="7.92 x 57mm Mauser">7.92mm</a> machine guns supplemented by two <a href="/wiki/MG_FF_cannon" title="MG FF cannon">20mm cannons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;nb 10&#93;</a></sup> The latter was much more effective than the .303; during the Battle it was not unknown for damaged German bombers to limp home with up to two hundred .303 hits.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> At some altitudes, the Bf 109 could outclimb the British fighter. It could also engage in vertical-plane negative-<i>g</i> manoeuvres without the engine cutting out because its <a href="/wiki/Daimler-Benz_DB_601" title="Daimler-Benz DB 601">DB 601</a> engine used <a href="/wiki/Gasoline_direct_injection" title="Gasoline direct injection">fuel injection</a>; this allowed the 109 to dive away from attackers more readily than the <a href="/wiki/Carburetor" title="Carburetor">carburettor</a>-equipped Merlin. On the other hand, the Bf 109E had a much larger turning circle than its two foes.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> In general, though, as Alfred Price noted in <i>The Spitfire Story</i>:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>...&#160;the differences between the Spitfire and the Me 109 in performance and handling were only marginal, and in a combat they were almost always surmounted by tactical considerations of which side had seen the other first, which had the advantage of sun, altitude, numbers, pilot ability, tactical situation, tactical co-ordination, amount of fuel remaining, etc.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bf-109E-3_at_Deutsches_Museum.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Bf-109E-3_at_Deutsches_Museum.png/220px-Bf-109E-3_at_Deutsches_Museum.png" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Bf-109E-3_at_Deutsches_Museum.png/330px-Bf-109E-3_at_Deutsches_Museum.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Bf-109E-3_at_Deutsches_Museum.png/440px-Bf-109E-3_at_Deutsches_Museum.png 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1068" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bf-109E-3_at_Deutsches_Museum.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Messerschmitt_Bf_109" title="Messerschmitt Bf 109">Messerschmitt Bf 109</a>E-3</div></div></div>\n<p>The Bf 109E was also used as a <i><a href="/wiki/Luftflotte_3#JABO_(Fighter-Bombers/Incursion_strike_groups)" title="Luftflotte 3">Jabo</a></i> (<i>jagdbomber</i>, <a href="/wiki/Fighter-bomber" title="Fighter-bomber">fighter-bomber</a>)\xe2\x80\x94the E-4/B and E-7 models could carry a 250&#160;kg bomb underneath the fuselage, the later model arriving during the battle. The Bf 109, unlike the <i><a href="/wiki/Junkers_Ju_87" title="Junkers Ju 87">Stuka</a></i>, could fight on equal terms with RAF fighters after releasing its <a href="/wiki/Ordnance_ammunition" class="mw-redirect" title="Ordnance ammunition">ordnance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the start of the battle, the twin-engined Messerschmitt Bf 110C long range <i>Zerst\xc3\xb6rer</i> ("Destroyer") was also expected to engage in air-to-air combat while escorting the <i>Luftwaffe</i> bomber fleet. Although the 110 was faster than the Hurricane and almost as fast as the Spitfire, its lack of manoeuvrability and acceleration meant that it was a failure as a long-range escort fighter. On 13 and 15 August, thirteen and thirty aircraft were lost, the equivalent of an entire <i>Gruppe</i>, and the type\'s worst losses during the campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> This trend continued with a further eight and fifteen lost on 16 and 17 August.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The most successful role of the Bf 110 during the battle was as a <i>Schnellbomber</i> (fast bomber). The Bf 110 usually used a shallow dive to bomb the target and escape at high speed.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_2000,_pp._257\xe2\x80\x93258._110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_2000,_pp._257\xe2\x80\x93258.-110">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weal_1999,_pp._42-51_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weal_1999,_pp._42-51-111">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> One unit, <a href="/wiki/Schnellkampfgeschwader_210#Origins_1940" title="Schnellkampfgeschwader 210"><i>Erprobungsgruppe 210</i></a> \xe2\x80\x93 initially formed as the service test unit (<i><a href="/wiki/Erprobungskommando" title="Erprobungskommando">Erprobungskommando</a></i>) for the emerging successor to the 110, the <a href="/wiki/Messerschmitt_Me_210" title="Messerschmitt Me 210">Me 210</a> \xe2\x80\x93 proved that the Bf 110 could still be used to good effect in attacking small or "pinpoint" targets.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_2000,_pp._257\xe2\x80\x93258._110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_2000,_pp._257\xe2\x80\x93258.-110">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boulton_Paul_Defiant.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Boulton_Paul_Defiant.jpg/220px-Boulton_Paul_Defiant.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Boulton_Paul_Defiant.jpg/330px-Boulton_Paul_Defiant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Boulton_Paul_Defiant.jpg/440px-Boulton_Paul_Defiant.jpg 2x" data-file-width="799" data-file-height="523" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boulton_Paul_Defiant.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Four <a href="/wiki/No._264_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 264 Squadron RAF">264 Squadron</a> Defiants (PS-V was shot down on 28 August 1940 over <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a> by Bf 109s.)</div></div></div>\n<p>The RAF\'s <a href="/wiki/Boulton_Paul_Defiant" title="Boulton Paul Defiant">Boulton Paul Defiant</a> had some initial success over Dunkirk because of its resemblance to the Hurricane; Luftwaffe fighters attacking from the rear were surprised by its unusual gun turret.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> During the Battle of Britain, it proved hopelessly outclassed. For various reasons, the Defiant lacked any form of forward-firing armament, and the heavy turret and second crewman meant it could not outrun or outmanoeuvre either the Bf 109 or Bf 110. By the end of August, after disastrous losses, the aircraft was withdrawn from daylight service.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ansell_pp._712\xe2\x80\x9314_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ansell_pp._712\xe2\x80\x9314-114">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bombers">Bombers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Bombers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Heinkel_He_111_during_the_Battle_of_Britain.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Heinkel_He_111_during_the_Battle_of_Britain.jpg/220px-Heinkel_He_111_during_the_Battle_of_Britain.jpg" width="220" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Heinkel_He_111_during_the_Battle_of_Britain.jpg/330px-Heinkel_He_111_during_the_Battle_of_Britain.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Heinkel_He_111_during_the_Battle_of_Britain.jpg/440px-Heinkel_He_111_during_the_Battle_of_Britain.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="676" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Heinkel_He_111_during_the_Battle_of_Britain.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Heinkel_He_111" title="Heinkel He 111">Heinkel He 111</a> bombers during the Battle of Britain</div></div></div>\n<p>The Luftwaffe\'s primary bombers were the <a href="/wiki/Heinkel_He_111" title="Heinkel He 111">Heinkel He 111</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dornier_Do_17" title="Dornier Do 17">Dornier Do 17</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Junkers_Ju_88" title="Junkers Ju 88">Junkers Ju 88</a> for level bombing at medium to high altitudes, and the <a href="/wiki/Junkers_Ju_87" title="Junkers Ju 87">Junkers Ju 87</a> <i>Stuka</i> for dive bombing tactics. The He 111 was used in greater numbers than the others during the conflict, and was better known, partly due to its distinctive wing shape. Each level bomber also had a few reconnaissance versions accompanying them that were used during the battle.<sup id="cite_ref-Price_1980,_pp.6-10_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Price_1980,_pp.6-10-115">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although it had been successful in previous Luftwaffe engagements, the <i>Stuka</i> suffered heavy losses in the Battle of Britain, particularly on 18 August, due to its slow speed and vulnerability to fighter interception after dive bombing a target. As the losses went up along with their limited payload and range, <i>Stuka</i> units were largely removed from operations over England and diverted to concentrate on shipping instead until they were eventually re-deployed to the Eastern Front in 1941. For some raids, they were called back, such as on 13 September to attack <a href="/wiki/RAF_Tangmere" title="RAF Tangmere">Tangmere airfield</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smith,_p._51_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith,_p._51-117">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The remaining three bomber types differed in their capabilities; the Heinkel 111 was the slowest; the Ju 88 was the fastest once its mainly external bomb load was dropped; and the Do 17 had the smallest bomb load.<sup id="cite_ref-Price_1980,_pp.6-10_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Price_1980,_pp.6-10-115">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> All three bomber types suffered heavy losses from the home-based British fighters, but the Ju 88 disproportionately so. The German bombers required constant protection by the Luftwaffe\'s fighter force. German escorts were not sufficiently numerous. <a href="/wiki/Messerschmitt_Bf_109" title="Messerschmitt Bf 109">Bf 109Es</a> were ordered to support more than 300\xe2\x80\x93400 bombers on any given day.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Later in the conflict, when night bombing became more frequent, all three were used. Due to its smaller bomb load, the lighter Do 17 was used less than the He 111 and Ju 88 for this purpose.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1969-094-18,_Dornier_Do_17_und_Supermarine_Spitfire.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1969-094-18%2C_Dornier_Do_17_und_Supermarine_Spitfire.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1969-094-18%2C_Dornier_Do_17_und_Supermarine_Spitfire.jpg" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1969-094-18%2C_Dornier_Do_17_und_Supermarine_Spitfire.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1969-094-18%2C_Dornier_Do_17_und_Supermarine_Spitfire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1969-094-18%2C_Dornier_Do_17_und_Supermarine_Spitfire.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1969-094-18%2C_Dornier_Do_17_und_Supermarine_Spitfire.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="512" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1969-094-18,_Dornier_Do_17_und_Supermarine_Spitfire.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>German propaganda photo purporting to show a Spitfire I flying very close to a Dornier 17Z<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;nb 11&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>On the British side, three bomber types were mostly used on night operations against targets such as factories, invasion ports and railway centres; the <a href="/wiki/Armstrong_Whitworth_Whitley" title="Armstrong Whitworth Whitley">Armstrong Whitworth Whitley</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Handley-Page_Hampden" class="mw-redirect" title="Handley-Page Hampden">Handley-Page Hampden</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Vickers_Wellington" title="Vickers Wellington">Vickers Wellington</a> were classified as heavy bombers by the RAF, although the Hampden was a medium bomber comparable to the He 111. The twin-engined <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Blenheim" title="Bristol Blenheim">Bristol Blenheim</a> and the obsolescent single-engined <a href="/wiki/Fairey_Battle" title="Fairey Battle">Fairey Battle</a> were both light bombers; the Blenheim was the most numerous of the aircraft equipping <a href="/wiki/RAF_Bomber_Command" title="RAF Bomber Command">RAF Bomber Command</a> and was used in attacks against shipping, ports, airfields and factories on the continent by day and by night. The Fairey Battle squadrons, which had suffered heavy losses in daylight attacks during the Battle of France, were brought up to strength with reserve aircraft and continued to operate at night in attacks against the invasion ports, until the Battle was withdrawn from UK front line service in October 1940.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pilots">Pilots</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Pilots">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RAF_Fighter_Command_1940_HU104450.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/RAF_Fighter_Command_1940_HU104450.jpg/220px-RAF_Fighter_Command_1940_HU104450.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/RAF_Fighter_Command_1940_HU104450.jpg/330px-RAF_Fighter_Command_1940_HU104450.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/RAF_Fighter_Command_1940_HU104450.jpg/440px-RAF_Fighter_Command_1940_HU104450.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="532" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RAF_Fighter_Command_1940_HU104450.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Spitfire pilot recounts how he shot down a Messerschmitt, <a href="/wiki/London_Biggin_Hill_Airport" title="London Biggin Hill Airport">Biggin Hill</a>, September 1940</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Group_Captain_A_G_Malan_WWII_IWM_CH_12661.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Group_Captain_A_G_Malan_WWII_IWM_CH_12661.jpg/170px-Group_Captain_A_G_Malan_WWII_IWM_CH_12661.jpg" width="170" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Group_Captain_A_G_Malan_WWII_IWM_CH_12661.jpg/255px-Group_Captain_A_G_Malan_WWII_IWM_CH_12661.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Group_Captain_A_G_Malan_WWII_IWM_CH_12661.jpg/340px-Group_Captain_A_G_Malan_WWII_IWM_CH_12661.jpg 2x" data-file-width="531" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Group_Captain_A_G_Malan_WWII_IWM_CH_12661.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/South_African" class="mw-redirect" title="South African">South African</a> <a href="/wiki/Adolph_Malan" title="Adolph Malan">Adolph "Sailor" Malan</a> led <a href="/wiki/No._74_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 74 Squadron RAF">No. 74 Squadron RAF</a> and was, at the time, the RAF\'s leading ace, with 27 planes destroyed, 7 shared, 2 unconfirmed, 3 probables and 16 damaged.</div></div></div>\n<p>Before the war, the RAF\'s processes for selecting potential candidates were opened to men of all social classes through the creation in 1936 of the <a href="/wiki/RAF_Volunteer_Reserve" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF Volunteer Reserve">RAF Volunteer Reserve</a>, which "...&#160;was designed to appeal, to&#160;... young men&#160;... without any class distinctions&#160;..."<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> The older squadrons of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Auxiliary_Air_Force" title="Royal Auxiliary Air Force">Royal Auxiliary Air Force</a> did retain some of their upper-class exclusiveness,<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> but their numbers were soon swamped by the newcomers of the RAFVR; by 1 September 1939, 6,646 pilots had been trained through the RAFVR.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By mid-1940, there were about 9,000 pilots in the RAF to man about 5,000 aircraft, most of which were bombers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources.
