(February 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Fighter Command was never short of pilots, but the problem of finding sufficient numbers of fully trained fighter pilots became acute by mid-August 1940.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>  With aircraft production running at 300 planes each week, only 200 pilots were trained in the same period. In addition, more pilots were allocated to squadrons than there were aircraft, as this allowed squadrons to maintain operational strength despite casualties and still provide for pilot leave.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_2000,_p._370_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_2000,_p._370-128">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Another factor was that only about 30% of the 9,000 pilots were assigned to operational squadrons; 20% of the pilots were involved in conducting pilot training, and a further 20% were undergoing further instruction, like those <a href="/wiki/List_of_British_Commonwealth_Air_Training_Plan_facilities_in_Canada" title="List of British Commonwealth Air Training Plan facilities in Canada">offered in Canada</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Rhodesia" title="Southern Rhodesia">Southern Rhodesia</a> to the Commonwealth trainees, although already qualified. The rest were assigned to staff positions, since RAF policy dictated that only pilots could make many staff and operational command decisions, even in engineering matters. At the height of fighting, and despite Churchill\'s insistence, only 30 pilots were released to the front line from administrative duties.<sup id="cite_ref-Ponting,_1940:_Myth_and_reality,_p.130_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ponting,_1940:_Myth_and_reality,_p.130-129">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;nb 12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For these reasons, and the permanent loss of 435 pilots during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_France" title="Battle of France">Battle of France</a> alone<sup id="cite_ref-short_hist_99_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-short_hist_99-47">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> along with many more wounded, and others lost in <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Norway">Norway</a>, the RAF had fewer experienced pilots at the start of the initial defence of their home. It was the lack of trained pilots in the fighting squadrons, rather than the <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_Aircraft_Production" title="Minister of Aircraft Production">lack of aircraft</a>, that became the greatest concern for Air Chief Marshal Hugh Dowding, Commander of Fighter Command. Drawing from regular RAF forces, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Auxiliary_Air_Force" title="Royal Auxiliary Air Force">Auxiliary Air Force</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force_Volunteer_Reserve" title="Royal Air Force Volunteer Reserve">Volunteer Reserve</a>, the British were able to muster some 1,103 fighter pilots on 1 July. Replacement pilots, with little flight training and often no gunnery training, suffered high casualty rates, thus exacerbating the problem.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_2000,_p._260._131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_2000,_p._260.-131">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Luftwaffe, on the other hand, were able to muster a larger number (1,450) of more experienced fighter pilots.<sup id="cite_ref-Ponting,_1940:_Myth_and_reality,_p.130_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ponting,_1940:_Myth_and_reality,_p.130-129">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Drawing from a cadre of <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a> veterans, these pilots already had comprehensive courses in aerial gunnery and instructions in tactics suited for fighter-versus-fighter combat.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_2000,_p._259._132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_2000,_p._259.-132">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Training manuals discouraged heroism, stressing the importance of attacking only when the odds were in the pilot\'s favour. Despite the high levels of experience, German fighter formations did not provide a sufficient reserve of pilots to allow for losses and leave,<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_2000,_p._370_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_2000,_p._370-128">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> and the Luftwaffe was unable to produce enough pilots to prevent a decline in operational strength as the battle progressed.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_participation">International participation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: International participation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;nb 13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Allies">Allies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Allies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dywizjon_303_4.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Dywizjon_303_4.jpg/220px-Dywizjon_303_4.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Dywizjon_303_4.jpg/330px-Dywizjon_303_4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Dywizjon_303_4.jpg/440px-Dywizjon_303_4.jpg 2x" data-file-width="614" data-file-height="436" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dywizjon_303_4.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>126 German aircraft or "Adolfs" were claimed by <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Polish</a> pilots of <a href="/wiki/303_Squadron" class="mw-redirect" title="303 Squadron">303 Squadron</a> during the Battle of Britain</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Non-British_personnel_in_the_RAF_during_the_Battle_of_Britain" title="Non-British personnel in the RAF during the Battle of Britain">Non-British personnel in the RAF during the Battle of Britain</a></div>\n<p>About 20% of pilots who took part in the battle were from non British countries. The Royal Air Force roll of honour for the Battle of Britain recognises 595 non-British pilots (out of 2,936) as flying at least one authorised operational <a href="/wiki/Sortie" title="Sortie">sortie</a> with an eligible unit of the RAF or <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Air_Arm" title="Fleet Air Arm">Fleet Air Arm</a> between 10 July and 31 October 1940.<sup id="cite_ref-Roll_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roll-17">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> These included 145 <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poles</a>, 127 <a href="/wiki/New_Zealander" class="mw-redirect" title="New Zealander">New Zealanders</a>, 112 <a href="/wiki/Canadians" title="Canadians">Canadians</a>, 88 <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovakia" title="Czechoslovakia">Czechoslovaks</a>, 10 Irish, 32 Australians, 28 <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgians</a>, 25 <a href="/wiki/Union_of_South_Africa" title="Union of South Africa">South Africans</a>, 13 French, 9 Americans, 3 <a href="/wiki/Southern_Rhodesia" title="Southern Rhodesia">Southern Rhodesians</a> and one each from <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mandatory_Palestine" title="Mandatory Palestine">Mandatory Palestine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> "Altogether in the fighter battles, the bombing raids, and the various patrols flown between 10 July and 31 October 1940 by the Royal Air Force, 1495 aircrew were killed, of whom 449 were fighter pilots, 718 aircrew from Bomber Command, and 280 from Coastal Command. Among those killed were 47 airmen from Canada, 24 from Australia, 17 from South Africa, 35 from Poland, 20 from Czechoslovakia and six from Belgium. Forty-seven New Zealanders lost their lives, including 15 fighter pilots, 24 bomber and eight coastal aircrew. The names of these Allied and Commonwealth airmen are inscribed in a memorial book which rests in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain_Chapel" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Britain Chapel">Battle of Britain Chapel</a> in Westminster Abbey. In the chapel is a stained glass window which contains the badges of the fighter squadrons which operated during the battle and the flags of the nations to which the pilots and aircrew belonged."<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>These pilots, some of whom had to flee their home countries because of the <a href="/wiki/Wehrmacht" title="Wehrmacht">Nazi war-machine</a>, fought with great distinction. The <a href="/wiki/No._303_Polish_Fighter_Squadron" title="No. 303 Polish Fighter Squadron">No. 303 Polish Fighter Squadron</a> for example was not just the highest scoring of the <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Hurricane" title="Hawker Hurricane">Hurricane</a> squadrons during the Battle of Britain, but also had the highest ratio of enemy aircraft destroyed to their own losses.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gretzyngier_and_Matusiak_p._25_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gretzyngier_and_Matusiak_p._25-139">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>"Had it not been for the magnificent material contributed by the Polish squadrons and their unsurpassed gallantry," wrote Air Chief Marshal Sir <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Dowding" title="Hugh Dowding">Hugh Dowding</a>, head of RAF Fighter Command, "I hesitate to say that the outcome of the Battle (of Britain) would have been the same."<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Axis">Axis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Axis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Corpo_Aereo_Italiano" title="Corpo Aereo Italiano">Corpo Aereo Italiano</a></div>\n<p>An element of the Italian Royal Air Force (<i><a href="/wiki/Regia_Aeronautica" title="Regia Aeronautica">Regia Aeronautica</a></i>) called the Italian Air Corps (<i>Corpo Aereo Italiano</i> or CAI) first saw action in late October 1940. It took part in the latter stages of the battle, but achieved limited success. The unit was redeployed in early 1941.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Luftwaffe_strategy">Luftwaffe strategy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Luftwaffe strategy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-13805,_Hermann_G%C3%B6ring.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-13805%2C_Hermann_G%C3%B6ring.jpg/170px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-13805%2C_Hermann_G%C3%B6ring.jpg" width="170" height="277" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-13805%2C_Hermann_G%C3%B6ring.jpg/255px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-13805%2C_Hermann_G%C3%B6ring.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-13805%2C_Hermann_G%C3%B6ring.jpg/340px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-13805%2C_Hermann_G%C3%B6ring.jpg 2x" data-file-width="491" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-13805,_Hermann_G%C3%B6ring.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hermann_G%C3%B6ring" title="Hermann G\xc3\xb6ring">Hermann G\xc3\xb6ring</a>, the commander of the Luftwaffe</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1987-121-30A,_Hugo_Sperrle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1987-121-30A%2C_Hugo_Sperrle.jpg/170px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1987-121-30A%2C_Hugo_Sperrle.jpg" width="170" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1987-121-30A%2C_Hugo_Sperrle.jpg/255px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1987-121-30A%2C_Hugo_Sperrle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1987-121-30A%2C_Hugo_Sperrle.jpg/340px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1987-121-30A%2C_Hugo_Sperrle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="567" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1987-121-30A,_Hugo_Sperrle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hugo_Sperrle" title="Hugo Sperrle">Hugo Sperrle</a>, the commander of <i><a href="/wiki/Luftflotte_3" title="Luftflotte 3">Luftflotte 3</a></i></div></div></div>\n<p>The high command\'s indecision over <a href="#German_aims_and_directives">which aim to pursue</a> was reflected in shifts in Luftwaffe strategy. Their Air War doctrine of concentrated <a href="/wiki/Close_air_support" title="Close air support">close air support</a> of the army at the battlefront succeeded in the <i><a href="/wiki/Blitzkrieg" title="Blitzkrieg">blitzkrieg</a></i> offensives against <a href="/wiki/Polish_September_Campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="Polish September Campaign">Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Operation_Weser%C3%BCbung" title="Operation Weser\xc3\xbcbung">Denmark and Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_France" title="Battle of France">the Low Countries and France</a>, but incurred significant losses. The Luftwaffe now had to establish or restore bases in the conquered territories, and rebuild their strength. In June 1940 they began regular armed reconnaissance flights and sporadic <i>St\xc3\xb6rangriffe</i>, nuisance raids of one or a few bombers, both day and night. These gave crews practice in navigation and avoiding air defences, and set off air raid alarms which disturbed civilian morale. Similar nuisance raids continued throughout the battle, into late 1940. Scattered <a href="/wiki/Naval_mine" title="Naval mine">naval mine</a>\xe2\x80\x93laying sorties began at the outset, and increased gradually over the battle period.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201367\xe2\x80\x9368,_71,_80,_92_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201367\xe2\x80\x9368,_71,_80,_92-141">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery200161\xe2\x80\x9362,_65\xe2\x80\x9366_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery200161\xe2\x80\x9362,_65\xe2\x80\x9366-142">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>G\xc3\xb6ring\'s operational directive of 30 June ordered destruction of the RAF as a whole, including the aircraft industry, with the aims of ending RAF bombing raids on Germany and facilitating attacks on ports and storage in the Luftwaffe blockade of Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-Murray_44-5_64-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murray_44-5-64">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Attacks on Channel shipping in the <i><a href="/wiki/Kanalkampf" title="Kanalkampf">Kanalkampf</a></i> began on 4 July, and were formalised on 11 July in an order by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Jeschonnek" title="Hans Jeschonnek">Hans Jeschonnek</a> which added the arms industry as a target.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBungay2000122_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBungay2000122-143">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBishop201082\xe2\x80\x9383_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBishop201082\xe2\x80\x9383-144">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 16 July Directive No. 16 ordered preparations for <a href="/wiki/Operation_Sea_Lion" title="Operation Sea Lion">Operation Sea Lion</a>, and on the next day the luftwaffe was ordered to stand by in full readiness. G\xc3\xb6ring met his air fleet commanders, and on 24 July issued "Tasks and Goals" of gaining <a href="/wiki/Air_supremacy" title="Air supremacy">air supremacy</a>, protecting the army and navy if invasion went ahead, and attacking the Royal Navy\'s ships as well as continuing the blockade. Once the RAF had been defeated, Luftwaffe bombers were to move forward beyond London without the need for fighter escort, destroying military and economic targets.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201372_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201372-67">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At a meeting on 1 August the command reviewed plans produced by each <i><a href="/wiki/Organization_of_the_Luftwaffe_(1933%E2%80%9345)#Fliegerkorps_and_Fliegerdivision" title="Organization of the Luftwaffe (1933\xe2\x80\x9345)">Fliegerkorps</a></i> with differing proposals for targets including whether to bomb airfields, but failed to focus priorities. Intelligence reports gave G\xc3\xb6ring the impression that the RAF was almost defeated: the intent was that raids would attract British fighters for the Luftwaffe to shoot down.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBungay2000123\xe2\x80\x93125_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBungay2000123\xe2\x80\x93125-145">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> On 6 August he finalised plans for this <a href="/wiki/Adlertag" title="Adlertag">"Operation Eagle Attack"</a> with <a href="/wiki/Albert_Kesselring" title="Albert Kesselring">Kesselring</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Sperrle" title="Hugo Sperrle">Sperle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hans-J%C3%BCrgen_Stumpff" title="Hans-J\xc3\xbcrgen Stumpff">Stumpff</a>: destruction of RAF Fighter Command across the south of England was to take four days, with lightly escorted small bomber raids leaving the main fighter force free to attack RAF fighters. Bombing of military and economic targets was then to systematically extend up to the Midlands until daylight attacks could proceed unhindered over the whole of Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery200156\xe2\x80\x9357,_61\xe2\x80\x9362_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery200156\xe2\x80\x9357,_61\xe2\x80\x9362-146">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201382\xe2\x80\x9383_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201382\xe2\x80\x9383-147">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bombing of London was to be held back while these night time "destroyer" attacks proceeded over other urban areas, then in culmination of the campaign a major attack on the capital was intended to cause a crisis when refugees fled London just as the <a href="/wiki/Operation_Sea_Lion" title="Operation Sea Lion">Operation Sea Lion</a> invasion was to begin.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201385_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201385-148">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> With hopes fading for the possibility of invasion, on 4 September Hitler authorised a main focus on day and night attacks on tactical targets with London as the main target, in what the British called <a href="/wiki/The_Blitz" title="The Blitz">the Blitz</a>. With increasing difficulty in defending bombers in day raids, the Luftwaffe shifted to a <a href="/wiki/Strategic_bombing" title="Strategic bombing">strategic bombing</a> campaign of night raids aiming to overcome British resistance by damaging infrastructure and food stocks, though intentional terror bombing of civilians was not sanctioned.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery200178\xe2\x80\x9389_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery200178\xe2\x80\x9389-149">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regrouping_of_Luftwaffe_in_Luftflotten">Regrouping of Luftwaffe in Luftflotten</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Regrouping of Luftwaffe in Luftflotten">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Luftwaffe was forced to regroup after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_for_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle for France">Battle of France</a> into three <i>Luftflotten</i> (Air Fleets) on Britain\'s southern and northern flanks. <a href="/wiki/Luftflotte_2" title="Luftflotte 2"><i>Luftflotte</i> 2</a>, commanded by <i>Generalfeldmarschall</i> Albert Kesselring, was responsible for the bombing of southeast England and the <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> area. <a href="/wiki/Luftflotte_3" title="Luftflotte 3"><i>Luftflotte</i> 3</a>, under <i>Generalfeldmarschall</i> <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Sperrle" title="Hugo Sperrle">Hugo Sperrle</a>, targeted the <a href="/wiki/West_Country" title="West Country">West Country</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, the Midlands, and northwest England. <a href="/wiki/Luftflotte_5" title="Luftflotte 5"><i>Luftflotte</i> 5</a>, led by <i><a href="/wiki/Generaloberst" title="Generaloberst">Generaloberst</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Hans-J%C3%BCrgen_Stumpff" title="Hans-J\xc3\xbcrgen Stumpff">Hans-J\xc3\xbcrgen Stumpff</a> from his headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, targeted the north of England and <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>. As the battle progressed, command responsibility shifted, with <i>Luftflotte</i> 3 taking more responsibility for the night-time <i><a href="/wiki/The_Blitz" title="The Blitz">Blitz</a></i> attacks while the main daylight operations fell upon <i>Luftflotte</i> 2\'s shoulders.\n</p><p>Initial Luftwaffe estimates were that it would take four days to defeat the RAF Fighter Command in southern England. This would be followed by a four-week offensive during which the bombers and long-range fighters would destroy all military installations throughout the country and wreck the British aircraft industry. The campaign was planned to begin with attacks on airfields near the coast, gradually moving inland to attack the ring of sector airfields defending London. Later reassessments gave the Luftwaffe five weeks, from 8 August to 15 September, to establish temporary air superiority over England.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_p._119_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_p._119-150">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> To achieve this goal, Fighter Command had to be destroyed, either on the ground or in the air, yet the Luftwaffe had to be able to preserve its own strength to be able to support the invasion; this meant that the Luftwaffe had to maintain a high "kill ratio" over the RAF fighters. The only alternative to the goal of air superiority was a <a href="/wiki/Terror_bombing" class="mw-redirect" title="Terror bombing">terror bombing</a> campaign aimed at the civilian population, but this was considered a last resort and it was (at this stage of the battle) expressly forbidden by Hitler.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_p._119_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_p._119-150">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Luftwaffe kept broadly to this scheme, but its commanders had differences of opinion on strategy. Sperrle wanted to eradicate the air defence infrastructure by bombing it. His counterpart, Kesselring, championed attacking London directly\xe2\x80\x94 either to bombard the British government into submission, or to draw RAF fighters into a decisive battle. G\xc3\xb6ring did nothing to resolve this disagreement between his commanders, and only vague directives were set down during the initial stages of the battle, with G\xc3\xb6ring seemingly unable to decide upon which strategy to pursue.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_2000,_p._122_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_2000,_p._122-151">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> He seemed at times obsessed with maintaining his own power base in the Luftwaffe and indulging his outdated beliefs on air fighting, which would later lead to tactical and strategic errors.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tactics">Tactics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Tactics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fighter_formations">Fighter formations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Fighter formations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Organization_of_the_Luftwaffe_(1933%E2%80%931945)" class="mw-redirect" title="Organization of the Luftwaffe (1933\xe2\x80\x931945)">Luftwaffe formations</a> employed a loose section of two (nicknamed the <a href="/wiki/Schwarm" class="mw-redirect" title="Schwarm"><i>Rotte</i></a> (pack)), based on a leader (<i>Rottenf\xc3\xbchrer</i>) followed at a distance of about 200 metres<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;nb 14&#93;</a></sup> by his wingman (nicknamed the <i>Rottenhund</i> (pack dog) or <i>Katschmarek</i><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>), who also flew slightly higher and was trained always to stay with his leader. With more room between them, both pilots could spend less time maintaining formation and more time looking around and covering each other\'s <a href="/wiki/Blind_spot_(vehicle)" class="mw-redirect" title="Blind spot (vehicle)">blind spots</a>. Attacking aircraft could be sandwiched between the two 109s.<sup id="cite_ref-Price_1980,_pp._12-13_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Price_1980,_pp._12-13-154">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;nb 15&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Rotte</i> allowed the <i>Rottenf\xc3\xbchrer</i> to concentrate on getting kills, but few wingmen had the chance,<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> leading to some resentment in the lower ranks where it was felt that the high scores came at their expense. Two sections were usually teamed up into a <i><a href="/wiki/Finger-four" title="Finger-four">Schwarm</a></i>, where all the pilots could watch what was happening around them. Each <i>Schwarm</i> in a <i>Staffel</i> flew at staggered heights and with about 200 metres of room between them, making the formation difficult to spot at longer ranges and allowing for a great deal of flexibility.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_2000,_p._259._132-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_2000,_p._259.-132">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> By using a tight "cross-over" turn, a <i>Schwarm</i> could quickly change direction.<sup id="cite_ref-Price_1980,_pp._12-13_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Price_1980,_pp._12-13-154">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Bf 110s adopted the same <i>Schwarm</i> formation as the 109s, but were seldom able to use this to the same advantage. The Bf 110\'s most successful method of attack was the "bounce" from above. When attacked, <i>Zerst\xc3\xb6rergruppen</i> increasingly resorted to forming large "<a href="/wiki/Lufbery_circle" title="Lufbery circle">defensive circles</a>", where each Bf 110 guarded the tail of the aircraft ahead of it. G\xc3\xb6ring ordered that they be renamed "offensive circles" in a vain bid to improve rapidly declining morale.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> These conspicuous formations were often successful in attracting RAF fighters that were sometimes "bounced" by high-flying Bf 109s. This led to the often repeated misconception that the Bf 110s were escorted by Bf 109s.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Higher-level_dispositions">Higher-level dispositions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Higher-level dispositions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Luftwaffe tactics were influenced by their fighters. The Bf 110 proved too vulnerable to the nimble single-engined RAF fighters. This meant the bulk of fighter escort duties fell on the Bf 109. Fighter tactics were then complicated by bomber crews who demanded closer protection. After the hard-fought battles of 15 and 18 August, G\xc3\xb6ring met with his unit leaders. During this conference, the need for the fighters to meet up on time with the bombers was stressed. It was also decided that one bomber <i>Gruppe</i> could only be properly protected by several <i>Gruppen</i> of 109s. In addition, G\xc3\xb6ring stipulated that as many fighters as possible were to be left free for <i>Freie Jagd</i> ("Free Hunts": a free-roving fighter sweep preceded a raid to try to sweep defenders out of the raid\'s path). The Ju 87 units, which had suffered heavy casualties, were only to be used under favourable circumstances.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> In early September, due to increasing complaints from the bomber crews about RAF fighters seemingly able to get through the escort screen, G\xc3\xb6ring ordered an increase in close escort duties. This decision shackled many of the Bf 109s to the bombers and, although they were more successful at protecting the bomber forces, casualties amongst the fighters mounted primarily because they were forced to fly and manoeuvre at reduced speeds.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Luftwaffe consistently varied its tactics in its attempts to break through the RAF defences. It launched many <i>Freie Jagd</i> to draw up RAF fighters. RAF fighter controllers were often able to detect these and position squadrons to avoid them, keeping to Dowding\'s plan to preserve fighter strength for the bomber formations. The Luftwaffe also tried using small formations of bombers as bait, covering them with large numbers of escorts. This was more successful, but escort duty tied the fighters to the bombers\' slow speed and made them more vulnerable.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_and_German_aircraft_a_dog_fight.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/British_and_German_aircraft_a_dog_fight.jpg/220px-British_and_German_aircraft_a_dog_fight.jpg" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/British_and_German_aircraft_a_dog_fight.jpg/330px-British_and_German_aircraft_a_dog_fight.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/British_and_German_aircraft_a_dog_fight.jpg/440px-British_and_German_aircraft_a_dog_fight.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="575" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_and_German_aircraft_a_dog_fight.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pattern of <a href="/wiki/Condensation_trails" class="mw-redirect" title="Condensation trails">condensation trails</a> left by British and German aircraft after a <a href="/wiki/Dogfight" title="Dogfight">dogfight</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>By September, standard tactics for raids had become an amalgam of techniques. A <i>Freie Jagd</i> would precede the main attack formations. The bombers would fly in at altitudes between 16,000 feet (4,900&#160;m) and 20,000 feet (6,100&#160;m), closely escorted by fighters. Escorts were divided into two parts (usually <i>Gruppen</i>), some operating in close contact with the bombers, and others a few hundred yards away and a little above. If the formation was attacked from the starboard, the starboard section engaged the attackers, the top section moving to starboard and the port section to the top position. If the attack came from the port side the system was reversed. British fighters coming from the rear were engaged by the rear section and the two outside sections similarly moving to the rear. If the threat came from above, the top section went into action while the side sections gained height to be able to follow RAF fighters down as they broke away. If attacked, all sections flew in defensive circles. These tactics were skilfully evolved and carried out, and were difficult to counter.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-2006-0123,_Adolf_Galland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-2006-0123%2C_Adolf_Galland.jpg/170px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-2006-0123%2C_Adolf_Galland.jpg" width="170" height="288" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-2006-0123%2C_Adolf_Galland.jpg/255px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-2006-0123%2C_Adolf_Galland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-2006-0123%2C_Adolf_Galland.jpg/340px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-2006-0123%2C_Adolf_Galland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="472" data-file-height="799" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-2006-0123,_Adolf_Galland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Adolf_Galland" title="Adolf Galland">Adolf Galland</a>, the successful leader of III./JG 26, became <i><a href="/wiki/Geschwaderkommodore" title="Geschwaderkommodore">Geschwaderkommodore</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/JG_26" class="mw-redirect" title="JG 26">JG 26</a> on 22 August</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Adolf_Galland" title="Adolf Galland">Adolf Galland</a> noted:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>We had the impression that, whatever we did, we were bound to be wrong. Fighter protection for bombers created many problems which had to be solved in action. Bomber pilots preferred close screening in which their formation was surrounded by pairs of fighters pursuing a zigzag course. Obviously, the visible presence of the protective fighters gave the bomber pilots a greater sense of security. However, this was a faulty conclusion, because a fighter can only carry out this purely defensive task by taking the initiative in the offensive. He must never wait until attacked because he then loses the chance of acting.\n</p><p>We fighter pilots certainly preferred the free chase during the approach and over the target area. This gives the greatest relief and the best protection for the bomber force.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>The biggest disadvantage faced by Bf 109 pilots was that without the benefit of long-range <a href="/wiki/Drop_tank" title="Drop tank">drop tanks</a> (which were introduced in limited numbers in the late stages of the battle), usually of 300 litres (66&#160;imp&#160;gal; 79&#160;US&#160;gal) capacity, the 109s had an <a href="/wiki/Combat_endurance" title="Combat endurance">endurance</a> of just over an hour and, for the 109E, a 600&#160;km (370&#160;mi) range. Once over Britain, a 109 pilot had to keep an eye on a red "low fuel" light on the instrument panel: once this was illuminated, he was forced to turn back and head for France. With the prospect of two long flights over water, and knowing their range was substantially reduced when escorting bombers or during combat, the <i>Jagdflieger</i> coined the term <i>Kanalkrankheit</i> or "Channel sickness".<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intelligence">Intelligence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Intelligence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Luftwaffe was ill-served by its lack of <a href="/wiki/Military_intelligence" title="Military intelligence">military intelligence</a> about the British defences.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_2000,_p._68_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_2000,_p._68-164">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>  The German intelligence services were fractured and plagued by rivalries; their performance was "amateurish".<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> By 1940, there were few German agents operating in Great Britain and a handful of bungled attempts to insert spies into the country were foiled.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As a result of intercepted radio transmissions, the Germans began to realise that the RAF fighters were being controlled from ground facilities; in July and August 1939, for example, the airship <i><a href="/wiki/LZ_130_Graf_Zeppelin" class="mw-redirect" title="LZ 130 Graf Zeppelin">Graf Zeppelin</a></i>, which was packed with equipment for listening in on RAF radio and RDF transmissions, flew around the coasts of Britain. Although the Luftwaffe correctly interpreted these new ground control procedures, they were incorrectly assessed as being rigid and ineffectual. A British <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a> system was well known to the Luftwaffe from intelligence gathered before the war, but the highly developed "<a href="/wiki/Dowding_system" title="Dowding system">Dowding system</a>" linked with fighter control had been a well-kept secret.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>  Even when good information existed, such as a November 1939 <i>Abwehr</i> assessment of Fighter Command strengths and capabilities by <i>Abteilung V</i>, it was ignored if it did not match conventional preconceptions.\n</p><p>On 16 July 1940, <i>Abteilung V</i>, commanded by <i><a href="/wiki/Oberstleutnant" title="Oberstleutnant">Oberstleutnant</a></i> "Beppo" Schmid, produced a report on the RAF and on Britain\'s defensive capabilities which was adopted by the frontline commanders as a basis for their operational plans. One of the most conspicuous failures of the report was the lack of information on the RAF\'s RDF network and control systems capabilities; it was assumed that the system was rigid and inflexible, with the RAF fighters being "tied" to their home bases.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_2000,_p._188._169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_2000,_p._188.-169">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16_July_report_on_RAF_defensive_capabilities_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16_July_report_on_RAF_defensive_capabilities-170">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> An optimistic and, as it turned out, erroneous conclusion reached was:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p><b>D. Supply Situation</b>...&#160;At present the British aircraft industry produces about 180 to 300 first line fighters and 140 first line bombers a month. In view of the present conditions relating to production (the appearance of raw material difficulties, the disruption or breakdown of production at factories owing to air attacks, the increased vulnerability to air attack owing to the fundamental reorganisation of the aircraft industry now in progress), it is believed that for the time being output will decrease rather than increase.\n</p><p>In the event of an intensification of air warfare it is expected that the present strength of the RAF will fall, and this decline will be aggravated by the continued decrease in production.<sup id="cite_ref-16_July_report_on_RAF_defensive_capabilities_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16_July_report_on_RAF_defensive_capabilities-170">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Because of this statement, reinforced by another more detailed report, issued on 10 August, there was a mindset in the ranks of the Luftwaffe that the RAF would run out of frontline fighters.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_2000,_p._188._169-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_2000,_p._188.-169">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> The Luftwaffe believed it was weakening Fighter Command at three times the actual attrition rate.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Many times, the leadership believed Fighter Command\'s strength had collapsed, only to discover that the RAF were able to send up defensive formations at will.\n</p><p>Throughout the battle, the Luftwaffe had to use numerous reconnaissance sorties to make up for the poor intelligence. Reconnaissance aircraft (initially mostly Dornier Do 17s, but increasingly Bf 110s) proved easy prey for British fighters, as it was seldom possible for them to be escorted by Bf 109s. Thus, the Luftwaffe operated "blind" for much of the battle, unsure of its enemy\'s true strengths, capabilities, and deployments. Many of the Fighter Command airfields were never attacked, while raids against supposed fighter airfields fell instead on bomber or coastal defence stations. The results of bombing and air fighting were consistently exaggerated, due to inaccurate claims, over-enthusiastic reports and the difficulty of confirmation over enemy territory. In the euphoric atmosphere of perceived victory, the Luftwaffe leadership became increasingly disconnected from reality. This lack of leadership and solid intelligence meant the Germans did not adopt consistent strategy, even when the RAF had its back to the wall. Moreover, there was never a systematic focus on one type of target (such as airbases, radar stations, or aircraft factories); consequently, the already haphazard effort was further diluted.<sup id="cite_ref-Allen_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allen-172">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Navigational_aids">Navigational aids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Navigational aids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>While the British were using radar for air defence more effectively than the Germans realised, the Luftwaffe attempted to press its own offensive with advanced <a href="/wiki/Radio_navigation" title="Radio navigation">radio navigation</a> systems of which the British were initially not aware. One of these was <i><a href="/wiki/Knickebein_(navigation)" class="mw-redirect" title="Knickebein (navigation)">Knickebein</a></i> ("bent leg"); this system was used at night and for raids where precision was required. It was rarely used during the Battle of Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Air-sea_rescue">Air-sea rescue</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Air-sea rescue">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Luftwaffe was much better prepared for the task of <a href="/wiki/Air-sea_rescue" title="Air-sea rescue">air-sea rescue</a> than the RAF, specifically tasking the <i><a href="/wiki/Seenotdienst" title="Seenotdienst">Seenotdienst</a></i> unit, equipped with about 30 <a href="/wiki/Heinkel_He_59" title="Heinkel He 59">Heinkel He 59</a> floatplanes, with picking up downed aircrew from the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Dover_Straits" class="mw-redirect" title="Dover Straits">Dover Straits</a>. In addition, Luftwaffe aircraft were equipped with life rafts and the aircrew were provided with sachets of a chemical called <a href="/wiki/Fluorescein" title="Fluorescein">fluorescein</a> which, on reacting with water, created a large, easy-to-see, bright green patch.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Richards_p._159_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards_p._159-175">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> In accordance with the <a href="/wiki/Geneva_Convention" class="mw-redirect" title="Geneva Convention">Geneva Convention</a>, the He 59s were unarmed and painted white with civilian registration markings and red crosses. Nevertheless, RAF aircraft attacked these aircraft, as some were escorted by Bf 109s.<sup id="cite_ref-Deere_1974,_p._89._176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deere_1974,_p._89.-176">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After single He 59s were forced to land on the sea by RAF fighters, on 1 and 9 July respectively,<sup id="cite_ref-Deere_1974,_p._89._176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deere_1974,_p._89.-176">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> a controversial order was issued to the RAF on 13 July; this stated that from 20 July, <i>Seenotdienst</i> aircraft were to be shot down. One of the reasons given by Churchill was:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>We did not recognise this means of rescuing enemy pilots so they could come and bomb our civil population again&#160;... all German air ambulances were forced down or shot down by our fighters on definite orders approved by the War Cabinet.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The British also believed that their crews would report on convoys,<sup id="cite_ref-Richards_p._159_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards_p._159-175">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Air_Ministry" title="Air Ministry">Air Ministry</a> issuing a communiqu\xc3\xa9 to the German government on 14 July that Britain was\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>unable, however, to grant immunity to such aircraft flying over areas in which operations are in progress on land or at sea, or approaching British or Allied territory, or territory in British occupation, or British or Allied ships. Ambulance aircraft which do not comply with the above will do so at their own risk and peril<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The white He 59s were soon repainted in camouflage colours and armed with defensive machine guns. Although another four He 59s were shot down by RAF aircraft,<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> the <i>Seenotdienst</i> continued to pick up downed Luftwaffe and Allied aircrew throughout the battle, earning praise from Adolf Galland for their bravery.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="RAF_strategy">RAF strategy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: RAF strategy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 100px"><div style="width: 100px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 98px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugh_Dowding.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Hugh_Dowding.jpg/147px-Hugh_Dowding.jpg" width="98" height="130" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Hugh_Dowding.jpg/220px-Hugh_Dowding.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Hugh_Dowding.jpg/294px-Hugh_Dowding.jpg 2x" data-file-width="951" data-file-height="1263" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Commander-in-Chief, Air Chief Marshal <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Dowding,_1st_Baron_Dowding" class="mw-redirect" title="Hugh Dowding, 1st Baron Dowding">Sir Hugh Dowding</a>\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 124px"><div style="width: 124px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 122px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Christopher_Quintin_Brand.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c4/Sir_Christopher_Quintin_Brand.jpg" width="122" height="130" data-file-width="150" data-file-height="160" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>10 Group Commander, Sir <a href="/wiki/Quintin_Brand" title="Quintin Brand">Quintin Brand</a>\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 98px"><div style="width: 98px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 96px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Keith_Park.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Sir_Keith_Park.jpg/144px-Sir_Keith_Park.jpg" width="96" height="130" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Sir_Keith_Park.jpg/217px-Sir_Keith_Park.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Sir_Keith_Park.jpg/288px-Sir_Keith_Park.jpg 2x" data-file-width="592" data-file-height="800" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>11 Group Commander, <a href="/wiki/Keith_Park" title="Keith Park">Keith Park</a>\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 124.66666666667px"><div style="width: 124.66666666667px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 122.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Air_Chf_Mshl_Leigh-Mallory.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Air_Chf_Mshl_Leigh-Mallory.jpg/184px-Air_Chf_Mshl_Leigh-Mallory.jpg" width="123" height="130" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Air_Chf_Mshl_Leigh-Mallory.jpg/276px-Air_Chf_Mshl_Leigh-Mallory.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Air_Chf_Mshl_Leigh-Mallory.jpg/368px-Air_Chf_Mshl_Leigh-Mallory.jpg 2x" data-file-width="744" data-file-height="789" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>12 Group Commander, <a href="/wiki/Trafford_Leigh-Mallory" title="Trafford Leigh-Mallory">Trafford Leigh-Mallory</a>\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 113.33333333333px"><div style="width: 113.33333333333px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 111.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Air_Vice-Marshal_Richard_Saul.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Air_Vice-Marshal_Richard_Saul.jpg/167px-Air_Vice-Marshal_Richard_Saul.jpg" width="112" height="130" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Air_Vice-Marshal_Richard_Saul.jpg/252px-Air_Vice-Marshal_Richard_Saul.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Air_Vice-Marshal_Richard_Saul.jpg/335px-Air_Vice-Marshal_Richard_Saul.jpg 2x" data-file-width="384" data-file-height="447" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>13 Group Commander, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Saul" title="Richard Saul">Richard Saul</a>\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Dowding_system">The Dowding system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: The Dowding system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dowding_system" title="Dowding system">Dowding system</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chain_home_coverage.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Chain_home_coverage.jpg/220px-Chain_home_coverage.jpg" width="220" height="360" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Chain_home_coverage.jpg/330px-Chain_home_coverage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Chain_home_coverage.jpg/440px-Chain_home_coverage.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="1048" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chain_home_coverage.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chain Home radar cover</div></div></div>\n<p>During early tests of the <a href="/wiki/Chain_Home" title="Chain Home">Chain Home</a> system, the slow flow of information from the CH radars and observers to the aircraft often caused them to miss their "bandits". The solution, today known as the "<a href="/wiki/Dowding_system" title="Dowding system">Dowding system</a>", was to create a set of reporting chains to move information from the various observation points to the pilots in their fighters. It was named after its chief architect, "Stuffy" Dowding.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Reports from CH radars and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Observer_Corps" title="Royal Observer Corps">Observer Corps</a> were sent directly to Fighter Command Headquarters (FCHQ) at Bentley Priory where they were "filtered" to combine multiple reports of the same formations into single tracks. Telephone operators would then forward only the information of interest to the Group headquarters, where the map would be re-created. This process was repeated to produce another version of the map at the Sector level, covering a much smaller area. Looking over their maps, Group level commanders could select squadrons to attack particular targets. From that point the Sector operators would give commands to the fighters to arrange an interception, as well as return them to base. Sector stations also controlled the <a href="/wiki/Anti-aircraft_warfare" title="Anti-aircraft warfare">anti-aircraft batteries</a> in their area; an army officer sat beside each fighter controller and directed the gun crews when to open and cease fire.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Dowding system dramatically improved the speed and accuracy of the information that flowed to the pilots. During the early war period it was expected that an average interception mission might have a 30% chance of ever seeing their target. During the battle, the Dowding system maintained an average rate over 75%, with several examples of 100% rates \xe2\x80\x93 every fighter dispatched found and intercepted its target.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In contrast, Luftwaffe fighters attempting to intercept raids had to randomly seek their targets and often returned home having never seen enemy aircraft. The result is what is now known as an example of "<a href="/wiki/Force_multiplication" title="Force multiplication">force multiplication</a>"; RAF fighters were as effective as two or more Luftwaffe fighters, greatly offsetting, or overturning, the disparity in actual numbers.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intelligence_2">Intelligence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Intelligence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Britain_map.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Battle_of_Britain_map.svg/220px-Battle_of_Britain_map.svg.png" width="220" height="276" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Battle_of_Britain_map.svg/330px-Battle_of_Britain_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Battle_of_Britain_map.svg/440px-Battle_of_Britain_map.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="841" data-file-height="1055" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Britain_map.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>RAF and <i>Luftwaffe</i> bases, group and <i>Luftflotte</i> boundaries, and range of <i>Luftwaffe</i> Bf 109 fighters. Southern part of British radar coverage: radar in North of Scotland not shown.</div></div></div>\n<p>While Luftwaffe intelligence reports underestimated British fighter forces and aircraft production, the British intelligence estimates went the other way: they overestimated German aircraft production, numbers and range of aircraft available, and numbers of Luftwaffe pilots. In action, the Luftwaffe believed from their pilot claims and the impression given by aerial reconnaissance that the RAF was close to defeat, and the British made strenuous efforts to overcome the perceived advantages held by their opponents.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201379\xe2\x80\x9380_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201379\xe2\x80\x9380-184">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>It is unclear how much the British intercepts of the <a href="/wiki/Enigma_cipher" class="mw-redirect" title="Enigma cipher">Enigma cipher</a>, used for high-security German radio communications, affected the battle. <a href="/wiki/Ultra_(cryptography)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ultra (cryptography)">Ultra</a>, the information obtained from Enigma intercepts, gave the highest echelons of the British command a view of German intentions. According to <a href="/wiki/F._W._Winterbotham" title="F. W. Winterbotham">F. W. Winterbotham</a>, who was the senior Air Staff representative in the Secret Intelligence Service,<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> Ultra helped establish the strength and composition of the Luftwaffe\'s formations, the aims of the commanders<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> and provided early warning of some raids.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> In early August it was decided that a small unit would be set up at FCHQ, which would process the flow of information from Bletchley and provide Dowding only with the most essential Ultra material; thus the Air Ministry did not have to send a continual flow of information to FCHQ, preserving secrecy, and Dowding was not inundated with non-essential information. Keith Park and his controllers were also told about Ultra.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> In a further attempt to camouflage the existence of Ultra, Dowding created a unit named <a href="/wiki/No._421_(Reconnaissance)_Flight_RAF" title="No. 421 (Reconnaissance) Flight RAF">No. 421 (Reconnaissance) Flight RAF</a>. This unit (which later became <a href="/wiki/No._91_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 91 Squadron RAF">No. 91 Squadron RAF</a>), was equipped with Hurricanes and Spitfires and sent out aircraft to search for and report Luftwaffe formations approaching England.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> In addition the radio listening service (known as <a href="/wiki/Y-stations" title="Y-stations">Y Service</a>), monitoring the patterns of Luftwaffe radio traffic contributed considerably to the early warning of raids.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Air-sea_rescue_2">Air-sea rescue</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Air-sea rescue">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force_Marine_Branch" title="Royal Air Force Marine Branch">Royal Air Force Marine Branch</a></div>\n<p>One of the biggest oversights of the entire system was the lack of adequate air-sea rescue organisation. The RAF had started organising a system in 1940 with High Speed Launches (HSLs) based on flying boat bases and at some overseas locations, but it was still believed that the amount of cross-Channel traffic meant that there was no need for a rescue service to cover these areas. Downed pilots and aircrew, it was hoped, would be picked up by any boats or ships which happened to be passing by. Otherwise the local life boat would be alerted, assuming someone had seen the pilot going into the water.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>RAF aircrew were issued with a life jacket, nicknamed the "<a href="/wiki/Personal_flotation_device" title="Personal flotation device">Mae West</a>," but in 1940 it still required manual inflation, which was almost impossible for someone who was injured or in shock. The waters of the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dover_Straits" class="mw-redirect" title="Dover Straits">Dover Straits</a> are cold, even in the middle of summer, and clothing issued to RAF aircrew did little to insulate them against these freezing conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_2000,_p._68_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_2000,_p._68-164">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> The RAF also imitated the German practice of issuing fluorescein.<sup id="cite_ref-Richards_p._159_175-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards_p._159-175">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> A conference in 1939 had placed air-sea rescue under Coastal Command. Because pilots had been lost at sea during the "Channel Battle", on 22 August, control of RAF rescue launches was passed to the local naval authorities and 12 <a href="/wiki/Westland_Lysander" title="Westland Lysander">Lysanders</a> were given to Fighter Command to help look for pilots at sea. In all some 200 pilots and aircrew were lost at sea during the battle. No proper air-sea rescue service was formed until 1941.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_2000,_p._68_164-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_2000,_p._68-164">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tactics_2">Tactics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Tactics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:19sqdn-spit1-1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/19sqdn-spit1-1.jpg/220px-19sqdn-spit1-1.jpg" width="220" height="98" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/19sqdn-spit1-1.jpg/330px-19sqdn-spit1-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/19sqdn-spit1-1.jpg/440px-19sqdn-spit1-1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="740" data-file-height="328" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:19sqdn-spit1-1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>X4474</i>, a late production Mk I Spitfire of <a href="/wiki/No._19_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 19 Squadron RAF">19 Squadron</a>, September 1940. During the battle 19 Squadron was part of the Duxford Wing</div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fighter_formations_2">Fighter formations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Fighter formations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In the late 1930s, Fighter Command expected to face only bombers over Britain, not single-engined fighters. A series of "Fighting Area Tactics" were formulated and rigidly adhered to, involving a series of manoeuvres designed to concentrate a squadron\'s firepower to bring down bombers. RAF fighters flew in tight, <a href="/wiki/Vic_formation" title="Vic formation">v-shaped sections</a> ("vics") of three aircraft, with four such "sections" in tight formation. Only the <a href="/wiki/Squadron_leader" title="Squadron leader">squadron leader</a> at the front was free to watch for the enemy; the other pilots had to concentrate on keeping station.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> Training also emphasised by-the-book attacks by sections breaking away in sequence. Fighter Command recognised the weaknesses of this structure early in the battle, but it was felt too risky to change tactics during the battle, because replacement pilots\xe2\x80\x94often with only minimal flying time\xe2\x80\x94could not be readily retrained,<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> and inexperienced pilots needed firm leadership in the air only rigid formations could provide.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> German pilots dubbed the RAF formations <i>Idiotenreihen</i> ("rows of idiots") because they left squadrons vulnerable to attack.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_2000,_p._260._131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_2000,_p._260.-131">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Front line RAF pilots were acutely aware of the inherent deficiencies of their own tactics. A compromise was adopted whereby squadron formations used much looser formations with one or two "weavers" flying independently above and behind to provide increased observation and rear protection; these tended to be the least experienced men and were often the first to be shot down without the other pilots even noticing that they were under attack.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_2000,_p._260._131-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_2000,_p._260.-131">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> During the battle, <a href="/wiki/No._74_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 74 Squadron RAF">74 Squadron</a> under Squadron Leader <a href="/wiki/Adolph_Malan" title="Adolph Malan">Adolph "Sailor" Malan</a> adopted a variation of the German formation called the "fours in line astern", which was a vast improvement on the old three aircraft "vic". Malan\'s formation was later generally used by Fighter Command.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Squadron-_and_higher-level_deployment">Squadron- and higher-level deployment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Squadron- and higher-level deployment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The weight of the battle fell upon 11 Group. Keith Park\'s tactics were to dispatch individual squadrons to intercept raids. The intention was to subject incoming bombers to continual attacks by relatively small numbers of fighters and try to break up the tight German formations. Once formations had fallen apart, stragglers could be picked off one by one. Where multiple squadrons reached a raid the procedure was for the slower Hurricanes to tackle the bombers while the more agile Spitfires held up the fighter escort. This ideal was not always achieved, resulting in occasions when Spitfires and Hurricanes reversed roles.<sup id="cite_ref-Orange_2001,_pp._96,_100_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Orange_2001,_pp._96,_100-197">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Park also issued instructions to his units to engage in frontal attacks against the bombers, which were more vulnerable to such attacks. Again, in the environment of fast moving, three-dimensional air battles, few RAF fighter units were able to attack the bombers from head-on.<sup id="cite_ref-Orange_2001,_pp._96,_100_197-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Orange_2001,_pp._96,_100-197">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the battle, some commanders, notably Leigh-Mallory, proposed squadrons be formed into "<a href="/wiki/Big_Wing" title="Big Wing">Big Wings</a>," consisting of at least three squadrons, to attack the enemy <i>en masse</i>, a method pioneered by <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Bader" title="Douglas Bader">Douglas Bader</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Squadron_Leader_Douglas_Bader,_CO_of_No._242_Squadron,_seated_on_his_Hawker_Hurricane_at_Duxford,_September_1940._CH1406.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Squadron_Leader_Douglas_Bader%2C_CO_of_No._242_Squadron%2C_seated_on_his_Hawker_Hurricane_at_Duxford%2C_September_1940._CH1406.jpg/310px-Squadron_Leader_Douglas_Bader%2C_CO_of_No._242_Squadron%2C_seated_on_his_Hawker_Hurricane_at_Duxford%2C_September_1940._CH1406.jpg" width="310" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Squadron_Leader_Douglas_Bader%2C_CO_of_No._242_Squadron%2C_seated_on_his_Hawker_Hurricane_at_Duxford%2C_September_1940._CH1406.jpg/465px-Squadron_Leader_Douglas_Bader%2C_CO_of_No._242_Squadron%2C_seated_on_his_Hawker_Hurricane_at_Duxford%2C_September_1940._CH1406.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Squadron_Leader_Douglas_Bader%2C_CO_of_No._242_Squadron%2C_seated_on_his_Hawker_Hurricane_at_Duxford%2C_September_1940._CH1406.jpg/620px-Squadron_Leader_Douglas_Bader%2C_CO_of_No._242_Squadron%2C_seated_on_his_Hawker_Hurricane_at_Duxford%2C_September_1940._CH1406.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3430" data-file-height="2480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Squadron_Leader_Douglas_Bader,_CO_of_No._242_Squadron,_seated_on_his_Hawker_Hurricane_at_Duxford,_September_1940._CH1406.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Douglas_Bader" title="Douglas Bader">Douglas Bader</a> commanded <a href="/wiki/No._242_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 242 Squadron RAF">242 Squadron</a> during the battle. He also led the Duxford Wing.</div></div></div>\n<p>Proponents of this tactic claimed interceptions in large numbers caused greater enemy losses while reducing their own casualties. Opponents pointed out the big wings would take too long to form up, and the strategy ran a greater risk of fighters being caught on the ground refuelling. The big wing idea also caused pilots to overclaim their kills, due to the confusion of a more intense battle zone. This led to the belief big wings were far more effective than they actually were.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The issue caused intense friction between Park and Leigh-Mallory, as 12 Group was tasked with protecting 11 Group\'s airfields whilst Park\'s squadrons intercepted incoming raids. The delay in forming up Big Wings meant the formations often did not arrive at all or until after German bombers had hit 11 Group\'s airfields.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> Dowding, to highlight the problem of the Big Wing\'s performance, submitted a report compiled by Park to the Air Ministry on 15 November. In the report, he highlighted that during the period of 11 September \xe2\x80\x93 31 October, the extensive use of the Big Wing had resulted in just 10 interceptions and one German aircraft destroyed, but his report was ignored.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> Post-war analysis agrees Dowding and Park\'s approach was best for 11 Group.\n</p><p>Dowding\'s removal from his post in November 1940 has been blamed on this struggle between Park and Leigh-Mallory\'s daylight strategy. The intensive raids and destruction wrought during the Blitz damaged both Dowding and Park in particular, for the failure to produce an effective night-fighter defence system, something for which the influential Leigh-Mallory had long criticised them.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bomber_and_Coastal_Command_contributions">Bomber and Coastal Command contributions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Bomber and Coastal Command contributions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RAFBristolBlenheimWWIIColour.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/RAFBristolBlenheimWWIIColour.jpg/220px-RAFBristolBlenheimWWIIColour.jpg" width="220" height="121" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/RAFBristolBlenheimWWIIColour.jpg/330px-RAFBristolBlenheimWWIIColour.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/66/RAFBristolBlenheimWWIIColour.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="220" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RAFBristolBlenheimWWIIColour.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Bristol Blenheim Mk IV of <a href="/wiki/No._21_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 21 Squadron RAF">21 Squadron</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/RAF_Bomber_Command" title="RAF Bomber Command">Bomber Command</a> and <a href="/wiki/RAF_Coastal_Command" title="RAF Coastal Command">Coastal Command</a> aircraft flew offensive sorties against targets in Germany and France during the battle.\n</p><p>An hour after the declaration of war, Bomber Command launched raids on warships and naval ports by day, and in night raids dropped leaflets as it was considered illegal to bomb targets which could affect civilians. After the initial disasters of the war, with <a href="/wiki/Vickers_Wellington" title="Vickers Wellington">Vickers Wellington</a> bombers shot down in large numbers attacking <a href="/wiki/Wilhelmshaven" title="Wilhelmshaven">Wilhelmshaven</a> and the slaughter of the <a href="/wiki/Fairey_Battle" title="Fairey Battle">Fairey Battle</a> squadrons sent to France, it became clear that they would have to operate mainly at night to avoid incurring very high losses.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> Churchill came to power on 10 May 1940, and night raids on German towns began with the <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6nchengladbach#Recent_history" title="M\xc3\xb6nchengladbach">bombing of M\xc3\xb6nchen-Gladbach</a> on the night of 11 May. The War Cabinet on 12 May agreed that German actions justified "unrestricted warfare", and on 14 May they authorised an attack on the night of 14/15 May against oil and rail targets in Germany. At the urging of <a href="/wiki/Clement_Attlee" title="Clement Attlee">Clement Attlee</a>, the Cabinet on 15 May authorised a full bombing strategy against "suitable military objectives", even where there could be civilian casualties. That evening, a night time bomber campaign began against the German oil industry, communications, and forests/crops, mainly in the <a href="/wiki/Ruhr" title="Ruhr">Ruhr area</a>. The RAF lacked accurate night navigation, and carried small bomb loads.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery2013241\xe2\x80\x93245_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery2013241\xe2\x80\x93245-203">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>\nAs the threat mounted, Bomber Command changed targeting priority on 3 June 1940 to attack the German aircraft industry. On 4 July, the Air Ministry gave Bomber Command orders to attack ports and shipping. By September, the build-up of invasion barges in the Channel ports had become a top priority target.<sup id="cite_ref-Halpenny_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halpenny-204">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 7 September, the government issued a warning that the invasion could be expected within the next few days and, that night, Bomber Command attacked the Channel ports and supply dumps. On 13 September, they carried out another large raid on the Channel ports, sinking 80 large barges in the port of <a href="/wiki/Ostend" title="Ostend">Ostend</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Taylor_and_Mayer_p.74_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor_and_Mayer_p.74-205">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> 84 barges were sunk in <a href="/wiki/Dunkirk" title="Dunkirk">Dunkirk</a> after another raid on 17 September and by 19 September, almost 200 barges had been sunk.<sup id="cite_ref-Halpenny_204-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halpenny-204">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> The loss of these barges may have contributed to Hitler\'s decision to postpone Operation <i>Sea Lion</i> indefinitely.<sup id="cite_ref-Halpenny_204-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Halpenny-204">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> The success of these raids was in part because the Germans had few <a href="/wiki/Freya_radar" title="Freya radar">Freya radar</a> stations set up in France, so that air defences of the French harbours were not nearly as good as the air defences over Germany;
