Bomber Command had directed some 60% of its strength against the Channel ports.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RAF_Bomber_Command_1940_HU104658.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/RAF_Bomber_Command_1940_HU104658.jpg/220px-RAF_Bomber_Command_1940_HU104658.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/RAF_Bomber_Command_1940_HU104658.jpg/330px-RAF_Bomber_Command_1940_HU104658.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/RAF_Bomber_Command_1940_HU104658.jpg/440px-RAF_Bomber_Command_1940_HU104658.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="520" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RAF_Bomber_Command_1940_HU104658.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Vickers_Wellington" title="Vickers Wellington">Wellington</a> crews studying maps at a briefing with the station commander, September 1940</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Blenheim" title="Bristol Blenheim">Bristol Blenheim</a> units also raided German-occupied airfields throughout July to December 1940, both during daylight hours and at night. Although most of these raids were unproductive, there were some successes; on 1 August, five out of twelve Blenheims sent to attack <a href="/wiki/Haamstede" title="Haamstede">Haamstede</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haren_Airport" title="Haren Airport">Evere</a> (<a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a>) were able to destroy or heavily damage three Bf 109s of II./JG 27 and apparently kill a <i><a href="/wiki/Staffelkapit%C3%A4n" title="Staffelkapit\xc3\xa4n">Staffelkapit\xc3\xa4n</a></i> identified as a <i><a href="/wiki/Hauptmann" title="Hauptmann">Hauptmann</a></i> Albrecht von Ankum-Frank. Two other 109s were claimed by Blenheim gunners.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;nb 16&#93;</a></sup> Another successful raid on Haamstede was made by a single Blenheim on 7 August which destroyed one 109 of 4./JG 54, heavily damaged another and caused lighter damage to four more.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BoulogneBarges1940.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/BoulogneBarges1940.jpg/220px-BoulogneBarges1940.jpg" width="220" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/BoulogneBarges1940.jpg/330px-BoulogneBarges1940.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/BoulogneBarges1940.jpg/440px-BoulogneBarges1940.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1189" data-file-height="1196" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BoulogneBarges1940.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>German invasion barges waiting at Boulogne Harbour, France during the Battle of Britain</div></div></div>\n<p>There were some missions which produced an almost 100% casualty rate amongst the Blenheims; one such operation was mounted on 13 August 1940 against a Luftwaffe airfield near <a href="/wiki/Aalborg" title="Aalborg">Aalborg</a> in north-eastern <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> by 12 aircraft of <a href="/wiki/No._82_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 82 Squadron RAF">82 Squadron</a>. One Blenheim returned early (the pilot was later charged and due to appear before a court martial, but was killed on another operation); the other eleven, which reached Denmark, were shot down, five by flak and six by Bf 109s. Of the 33 crewmen who took part in the attack, 20 were killed and 13 captured.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As well as the bombing operations, Blenheim-equipped units had been formed to carry out long-range strategic reconnaissance missions over Germany and German-occupied territories. In this role, the Blenheims again proved to be too slow and vulnerable against Luftwaffe fighters, and they took constant casualties.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (March 2015)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Coastal Command directed its attention towards the protection of British shipping, and the destruction of enemy shipping. As invasion became more likely, it participated in the strikes on French harbours and airfields, laying mines, and mounting numerous reconnaissance missions over the enemy-held coast. In all, some 9,180 sorties were flown by bombers from July to October 1940. Although this was much less than the 80,000 sorties flown by fighters, bomber crews suffered about half the total casualties borne by their fighter colleagues. The bomber contribution was, therefore, much more dangerous on a loss-per-sortie comparison.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bomber, reconnaissance, and antisubmarine patrol operations continued throughout these months with little respite and none of the publicity accorded to Fighter Command. In his famous 20 August speech about "<a href="/wiki/The_Few" title="The Few">The Few</a>", praising Fighter Command, Churchill also made a point of mentioning Bomber Command\'s contribution, adding that bombers were even then striking back at Germany; this part of the speech is often overlooked, even today.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Speech_of_20_August_1940_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Speech_of_20_August_1940-213">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain_Chapel" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Britain Chapel">Battle of Britain Chapel</a> in <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> lists in a roll of honour, 718 Bomber Command crew members, and 280 from Coastal Command who were killed between 10 July and 31 October.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bomber and Coastal Command attacks against invasion barge concentrations in Channel ports were widely reported by the British media during September and October 1940.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> In what became known as \'the Battle of the Barges\' RAF attacks were claimed in British propaganda to have sunk large numbers of barges, and to have created widespread chaos and disruption to German invasion preparations. Given the volume of British propaganda interest in these bomber attacks during September and earlier October, it is striking how quickly this was overlooked once the Battle of Britain had been concluded. Even by mid-war the bomber pilots\' efforts had been largely eclipsed by a continuing focus on the Few, this a result of the Air Ministry\'s continuing valorisation of the \xe2\x80\xb3fighter boys\xe2\x80\xb3, beginning with the March 1941 Battle of Britain propaganda pamphlet.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phases_of_the_battle">Phases of the battle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Phases of the battle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-0678,_Flugzeuge_Heinkel_He_111.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-0678%2C_Flugzeuge_Heinkel_He_111.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-0678%2C_Flugzeuge_Heinkel_He_111.jpg" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-0678%2C_Flugzeuge_Heinkel_He_111.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-0678%2C_Flugzeuge_Heinkel_He_111.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-0678%2C_Flugzeuge_Heinkel_He_111.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-0678%2C_Flugzeuge_Heinkel_He_111.jpg 2x" data-file-width="793" data-file-height="571" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_141-0678,_Flugzeuge_Heinkel_He_111.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>German Heinkel He 111 bombers over the English Channel 1940</div></div></div>\n<p>The battle covered a shifting geographical area, and there have been differing opinions on significant dates: when the <a href="/wiki/Air_Ministry" title="Air Ministry">Air Ministry</a> proposed 8 August as the start, Dowding responded that operations "merged into one another almost insensibly", and proposed 10 July as the onset of increased attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery200161\xe2\x80\x9362_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery200161\xe2\x80\x9362-217">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> With the caution that phases drifted into each other and dates are not firm, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force_Museum" title="Royal Air Force Museum">Royal Air Force Museum</a> states that five main phases can be identified:<sup id="cite_ref-RAF_Museum_phases_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAF_Museum_phases-218">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>26 June \xe2\x80\x93 16 July: <i>St\xc3\xb6rangriffe</i> ("nuisance raids"), scattered small scale probing attacks both day and night, armed reconnaissance and mine-laying sorties. From 4 July, daylight <i><a href="/wiki/Kanalkampf" title="Kanalkampf">Kanalkampf</a></i> ("<a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">the Channel</a> battles") against shipping.</li>\n<li>17 July \xe2\x80\x93 12 August: daylight <i>Kanalkampf</i> attacks on shipping intensify through this period, increased attacks on ports and coastal airfields, night raids on RAF and aircraft manufacturing.</li>\n<li>13 August \xe2\x80\x93 6 September: <i><a href="/wiki/Adlerangriff" class="mw-redirect" title="Adlerangriff">Adlerangriff</a></i> ("Eagle Attack"), the main assault; attempt to destroy the RAF in southern England, including massive daylight attacks on RAF airfields, followed from 19 August by heavy night bombing of ports and industrial cities, including suburbs of London.</li>\n<li>7 September \xe2\x80\x93 2 October: <a href="/wiki/The_Blitz" title="The Blitz">the Blitz</a> commences, main focus day and night attacks on London.</li>\n<li>3\xe2\x80\x9331 October: large scale night bombing raids, mostly on London; daylight attacks now confined to small scale fighter-bomber <i>St\xc3\xb6rangriffe</i> raids luring RAF fighters into dogfights.</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Small_scale_raids">Small scale raids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Small scale raids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The RAF night bombing campaign against military objectives in German towns <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6nchengladbach#Recent_history" title="M\xc3\xb6nchengladbach">began on 11 May</a>. The small forces available were given ambitious objectives, but lacked night navigation capability and their isolated inaccurate attacks were thought by the Germans to be intended to terrorise civilians. From 4 July the RAF achieved some successes with raids on Channel ports, anticipating the build up for an invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery2013241\xe2\x80\x93245_203-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery2013241\xe2\x80\x93245-203">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery200163\xe2\x80\x9365_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery200163\xe2\x80\x9365-219">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following Germany\'s rapid territorial gains in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_France" title="Battle of France">Battle of France</a>, the Luftwaffe had to reorganise its forces, set up bases along the coast, and rebuild after heavy losses. It began small scale bombing raids on Britain on the night of 5/6 June, and continued sporadic attacks throughout June and July.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery200147\xe2\x80\x9349,_61_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery200147\xe2\x80\x9349,_61-220">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> The first large-scale attack was at night, on 18/19 June, when small raids scattered between Yorkshire and Kent involved in total 100 bombers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBishop201054_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBishop201054-221">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> These <i>St\xc3\xb6rangriffe</i> ("nuisance raids") which involved only a few aeroplanes, sometimes just one, were used to train bomber crews in both day and night attacks, to test defences and try out methods, with most flights at night. They found that, rather than carrying small numbers of large high explosive bombs, it was more effective to use more small bombs, similarly incendiaries had to cover a large area to set effective fires. These training flights continued through August and into the first week of September.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201371\xe2\x80\x9372_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201371\xe2\x80\x9372-222">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> Against this, the raids also gave the British time to assess the German tactics, and invaluable time for the RAF fighters and anti-aircraft defences to prepare and gain practice.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery200166_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery200166-223">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Interior_of_RAF_Fighter_Command%27s_Sector_%27G%27_Operations_Room_at_Duxford,_Cambridgeshire,_September_1940._CH1401.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Interior_of_RAF_Fighter_Command%27s_Sector_%27G%27_Operations_Room_at_Duxford%2C_Cambridgeshire%2C_September_1940._CH1401.jpg/220px-Interior_of_RAF_Fighter_Command%27s_Sector_%27G%27_Operations_Room_at_Duxford%2C_Cambridgeshire%2C_September_1940._CH1401.jpg" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Interior_of_RAF_Fighter_Command%27s_Sector_%27G%27_Operations_Room_at_Duxford%2C_Cambridgeshire%2C_September_1940._CH1401.jpg/330px-Interior_of_RAF_Fighter_Command%27s_Sector_%27G%27_Operations_Room_at_Duxford%2C_Cambridgeshire%2C_September_1940._CH1401.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Interior_of_RAF_Fighter_Command%27s_Sector_%27G%27_Operations_Room_at_Duxford%2C_Cambridgeshire%2C_September_1940._CH1401.jpg/440px-Interior_of_RAF_Fighter_Command%27s_Sector_%27G%27_Operations_Room_at_Duxford%2C_Cambridgeshire%2C_September_1940._CH1401.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3384" data-file-height="2480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Interior_of_RAF_Fighter_Command%27s_Sector_%27G%27_Operations_Room_at_Duxford,_Cambridgeshire,_September_1940._CH1401.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Interior of RAF Fighter Command\'s <a href="/wiki/RAF_Fighter_Command_Order_of_Battle_1940" title="RAF Fighter Command Order of Battle 1940">Sector \'G\'</a> Operations Room at <a href="/wiki/Duxford_Aerodrome" title="Duxford Aerodrome">Duxford</a>, 1940</div></div></div>\n<p>The attacks were widespread: over the night of 30 June alarms were set off in 20 counties by just 20 bombers, then next day the first daylight raids occurred during 1 July, on both <a href="/wiki/Kingston_upon_Hull" title="Kingston upon Hull">Hull in Yorkshire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wick,_Caithness" title="Wick, Caithness">Wick, Caithness</a>. On 3 July most flights were reconnaissance sorties, but 15 civilians were killed when bombs hit <a href="/wiki/Guildford" title="Guildford">Guildford</a> in Surrey.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBishop201080\xe2\x80\x9381_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBishop201080\xe2\x80\x9381-224">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> Numerous small <i>St\xc3\xb6rangriffe</i> raids, both day and night, were made daily through August, September and into the winter, with aims including bringing RAF fighters up to battle, destruction of specific military and economic targets, and setting off air-raid warnings to affect civilian morale: four major air-raids in August involved hundreds of bombers, in the same month 1,062 small raids were made, spread across the whole of Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201380_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201380-225">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Channel_battles">Channel battles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Channel battles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Kanalkampf" title="Kanalkampf">Kanalkampf</a></i> comprised a series of running fights over convoys in the English Channel. It was launched partly because Kesselring and <a href="/wiki/Sperrle" title="Sperrle">Sperrle</a> were not sure about what else to do, and partly because it gave German aircrews some training and a chance to probe the British defences.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_2000,_p._122_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_2000,_p._122-151">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Dowding could provide only minimal shipping protection, and these battles off the coast tended to favour the Germans, whose bomber escorts had the advantage of altitude and outnumbered the RAF fighters. From 9 July reconnaissance probing by <a href="/wiki/Dornier_Do_17" title="Dornier Do 17">Dornier Do 17</a> bombers put a severe strain on RAF pilots and machines, with high RAF losses to Bf 109s. When nine <a href="/wiki/No._141_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 141 Squadron RAF">141 Squadron</a> <a href="/wiki/Boulton_Paul_Defiant" title="Boulton Paul Defiant">Defiants</a> went into action on 19 July six were lost to Bf 109s before a squadron of <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Hurricane" title="Hawker Hurricane">Hurricanes</a> intervened. On 25 July a coal convoy and escorting destroyers suffered such heavy losses to attacks by <a href="/wiki/Junkers_Ju87" class="mw-redirect" title="Junkers Ju87">Stuka</a> <a href="/wiki/Dive_bomber" title="Dive bomber">dive bombers</a> that the <a href="/wiki/Admiralty" title="Admiralty">Admiralty</a> decided convoys should travel at night: the RAF shot down 16 raiders but lost 7 aircraft. By 8 August 18 coal ships and 4 destroyers had been sunk, but the Navy was determined to send a convoy of 20 ships through rather than move the coal by railway. After repeated Stuka attacks that day, six ships were badly damaged, four were sunk and only four reached their destination. The RAF lost 19 fighters and shot down 31 German aircraft. The Navy now cancelled all further convoys through the Channel and sent the cargo by rail. Even so, these early combat encounters provided both sides with experience.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Main_assault">Main assault</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Main assault">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The main attack upon the RAF\'s defences was code-named <i>Adlerangriff</i> ("Eagle Attack"). Intelligence reports gave G\xc3\xb6ring the impression that the RAF was almost defeated, and raids would attract British fighters for the Luftwaffe to shoot down.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBungay2000123\xe2\x80\x93125_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBungay2000123\xe2\x80\x93125-145">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> The strategy agreed on 6 August was to destroy RAF Fighter Command across the south of England in four days, then bombing of military and economic targets was to systematically extend up to the Midlands until daylight attacks could proceed unhindered over the whole of Britain, culminating in a major bombing attack on London.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery200156\xe2\x80\x9357,_61\xe2\x80\x9362_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery200156\xe2\x80\x9357,_61\xe2\x80\x9362-146">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201382\xe2\x80\x9383,_85_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201382\xe2\x80\x9383,_85-227">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Assault_on_RAF:_radar_and_airfields">Assault on RAF: radar and airfields</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Assault on RAF: radar and airfields">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:East_Coast_Chain_Home_radar_station_CH15176.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/East_Coast_Chain_Home_radar_station_CH15176.jpg/220px-East_Coast_Chain_Home_radar_station_CH15176.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/East_Coast_Chain_Home_radar_station_CH15176.jpg/330px-East_Coast_Chain_Home_radar_station_CH15176.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/East_Coast_Chain_Home_radar_station_CH15176.jpg/440px-East_Coast_Chain_Home_radar_station_CH15176.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:East_Coast_Chain_Home_radar_station_CH15176.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>East Coast <a href="/wiki/Chain_Home" title="Chain Home">Chain Home</a> radar station</div></div></div>\n<p>Poor weather delayed <i><a href="/wiki/Adlertag" title="Adlertag">Adlertag</a></i> ("Eagle Day") until 13 August 1940. On 12 August, the first attempt was made to blind the Dowding system, when aircraft from the specialist fighter-bomber unit <i>Erprobungsgruppe</i> 210 attacked four <a href="/wiki/Chain_Home" title="Chain Home">radar stations</a>. Three were briefly taken off the air but were back working within six hours.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay,_2000,_pp._203\xe2\x80\x93205_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay,_2000,_pp._203\xe2\x80\x93205-228">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup>  The raids appeared to show that British radars were difficult to knock out. The failure to mount follow-up attacks allowed the RAF to get the stations back on the air, and the Luftwaffe neglected strikes on the supporting infrastructure, such as phone lines and power stations, which could have rendered the radars useless, even if the towers themselves (which were very difficult to destroy) remained intact.<sup id="cite_ref-Allen_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allen-172">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Adlertag</i> opened with a series of attacks, led again by <i>Erpro</i> 210,<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay,_2000,_pp._203\xe2\x80\x93205_228-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay,_2000,_pp._203\xe2\x80\x93205-228">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> on coastal airfields used as forward landing grounds for the RAF fighters, as well as \'satellite airfields\'<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;nb 17&#93;</a></sup> (including <a href="/wiki/RAF_Manston" title="RAF Manston">Manston</a> and <a href="/wiki/RAF_Hawkinge" title="RAF Hawkinge">Hawkinge</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay,_2000,_pp._203\xe2\x80\x93205_228-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay,_2000,_pp._203\xe2\x80\x93205-228">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> As the week drew on, the airfield attacks moved further inland, and repeated raids were made on the radar chain. 15 August was "The Greatest Day" when the Luftwaffe mounted the largest number of sorties of the campaign. <i>Luftflotte</i> 5 attacked the north of England. Believing Fighter Command strength to be concentrated in the south, raiding forces from <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> ran into unexpectedly strong resistance. Inadequately escorted by Bf 110s, bombers were shot down in large numbers. North East England was attacked by 65 Heinkel 111s escorted by 34 Messerschmitt 110s, and <a href="/wiki/RAF_Driffield" title="RAF Driffield">RAF Great Driffield</a> was attacked by 50 unescorted Junkers 88s. Out of 115 bombers and 35 fighters sent, 75 planes were destroyed and many others damaged beyond repair. Furthermore, due to early engagement by RAF fighters many of the bombers dropped their payloads ineffectively early.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> As a result of these casualties, <i>Luftflotte</i> 5 did not appear in strength again in the campaign.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pilots_of_No._310_(Czechoslovak)_Squadron_RAF_in_front_of_Hawker_Hurricane_Mk_I_at_Duxford,_Cambridgeshire,_7_September_1940._CH1299.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Pilots_of_No._310_%28Czechoslovak%29_Squadron_RAF_in_front_of_Hawker_Hurricane_Mk_I_at_Duxford%2C_Cambridgeshire%2C_7_September_1940._CH1299.jpg/220px-Pilots_of_No._310_%28Czechoslovak%29_Squadron_RAF_in_front_of_Hawker_Hurricane_Mk_I_at_Duxford%2C_Cambridgeshire%2C_7_September_1940._CH1299.jpg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Pilots_of_No._310_%28Czechoslovak%29_Squadron_RAF_in_front_of_Hawker_Hurricane_Mk_I_at_Duxford%2C_Cambridgeshire%2C_7_September_1940._CH1299.jpg/330px-Pilots_of_No._310_%28Czechoslovak%29_Squadron_RAF_in_front_of_Hawker_Hurricane_Mk_I_at_Duxford%2C_Cambridgeshire%2C_7_September_1940._CH1299.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Pilots_of_No._310_%28Czechoslovak%29_Squadron_RAF_in_front_of_Hawker_Hurricane_Mk_I_at_Duxford%2C_Cambridgeshire%2C_7_September_1940._CH1299.jpg/440px-Pilots_of_No._310_%28Czechoslovak%29_Squadron_RAF_in_front_of_Hawker_Hurricane_Mk_I_at_Duxford%2C_Cambridgeshire%2C_7_September_1940._CH1299.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2480" data-file-height="1752" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pilots_of_No._310_(Czechoslovak)_Squadron_RAF_in_front_of_Hawker_Hurricane_Mk_I_at_Duxford,_Cambridgeshire,_7_September_1940._CH1299.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Czechoslovakia" title="Czechoslovakia">Czechoslovak</a> fighter pilots of <a href="/wiki/No._310_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 310 Squadron RAF">No. 310 Squadron RAF</a> at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Duxford" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF Duxford">RAF Duxford</a> in 1940</div></div></div>\n<p>18 August, which had the greatest number of casualties to both sides, has been dubbed "<a href="/wiki/The_Hardest_Day" title="The Hardest Day">The Hardest Day</a>". Following this grinding battle, exhaustion and the weather reduced operations for most of a week, allowing the Luftwaffe to review their performance. "The Hardest Day" had sounded the end for the Ju 87 in the campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> This veteran of <i>Blitzkrieg</i> was too vulnerable to fighters to operate over Britain. So as to preserve the <i>Stuka</i> force, G\xc3\xb6ring withdrew them from the fighting. This removed the main Luftwaffe precision-bombing weapon and shifted the burden of pinpoint attacks on the already-stretched <i>Erpro</i> 210. The Bf 110 proved too clumsy for dogfighting with single-engined fighters, and its participation was scaled back. It would be used only when range required it or when sufficient single-engined escort could not be provided for the bombers.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pilots_of_No._19_Squadron_RAF_relax_in_the_crew_room_at_Fowlmere,_the_satellite_airfield_to_Duxford_in_Cambridgeshire,_September_1940._CH1461.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Pilots_of_No._19_Squadron_RAF_relax_in_the_crew_room_at_Fowlmere%2C_the_satellite_airfield_to_Duxford_in_Cambridgeshire%2C_September_1940._CH1461.jpg/220px-Pilots_of_No._19_Squadron_RAF_relax_in_the_crew_room_at_Fowlmere%2C_the_satellite_airfield_to_Duxford_in_Cambridgeshire%2C_September_1940._CH1461.jpg" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Pilots_of_No._19_Squadron_RAF_relax_in_the_crew_room_at_Fowlmere%2C_the_satellite_airfield_to_Duxford_in_Cambridgeshire%2C_September_1940._CH1461.jpg/330px-Pilots_of_No._19_Squadron_RAF_relax_in_the_crew_room_at_Fowlmere%2C_the_satellite_airfield_to_Duxford_in_Cambridgeshire%2C_September_1940._CH1461.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Pilots_of_No._19_Squadron_RAF_relax_in_the_crew_room_at_Fowlmere%2C_the_satellite_airfield_to_Duxford_in_Cambridgeshire%2C_September_1940._CH1461.jpg/440px-Pilots_of_No._19_Squadron_RAF_relax_in_the_crew_room_at_Fowlmere%2C_the_satellite_airfield_to_Duxford_in_Cambridgeshire%2C_September_1940._CH1461.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2470" data-file-height="1772" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pilots_of_No._19_Squadron_RAF_relax_in_the_crew_room_at_Fowlmere,_the_satellite_airfield_to_Duxford_in_Cambridgeshire,_September_1940._CH1461.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pilots of <a href="/wiki/No._19_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 19 Squadron RAF">No. 19 Squadron RAF</a> relax in the crew room at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Fowlmere" title="RAF Fowlmere">RAF Fowlmere</a>, 1940</div></div></div>\n<p>G\xc3\xb6ring made yet another important decision: to order more bomber escorts at the expense of free-hunting sweeps. To achieve this, the weight of the attack now fell on <i>Luftflotte</i> 2, and the bulk of the Bf 109s in <i><a href="/wiki/Luftflotte_3" title="Luftflotte 3">Luftflotte 3</a></i> were transferred to Kesselring\'s command, reinforcing the fighter bases in the <a href="/wiki/Pas-de-Calais" title="Pas-de-Calais">Pas-de-Calais</a>. Stripped of its fighters, <i>Luftflotte 3</i> would concentrate on the night bombing campaign. G\xc3\xb6ring, expressing disappointment with the fighter performance thus far in the campaign, also made sweeping changes in the command structure of the fighter units, replacing many <i><a href="/wiki/Geschwaderkommodore" title="Geschwaderkommodore">Geschwaderkommodore</a></i> with younger, more aggressive pilots like Adolf Galland and <a href="/wiki/Werner_M%C3%B6lders" title="Werner M\xc3\xb6lders">Werner M\xc3\xb6lders</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Finally, G\xc3\xb6ring stopped the attacks on the radar chain. These were seen as unsuccessful, and neither the <i><a href="/wiki/Reichsmarschall" title="Reichsmarschall">Reichsmarschall</a></i> nor his subordinates realised how vital the Chain Home stations were to the defence systems. It was known that radar provided some early warning of raids, but the belief among German fighter pilots was that anything bringing up the "<a href="/wiki/Tommy_Atkins" title="Tommy Atkins">Tommies</a>" to fight was to be encouraged.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Raids_on_British_cities">Raids on British cities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Raids on British cities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>On the afternoon of 15 August, <i>Hauptmann</i> <a href="/wiki/Walter_Rubensd%C3%B6rffer" class="mw-redirect" title="Walter Rubensd\xc3\xb6rffer">Walter Rubensd\xc3\xb6rffer</a> leading <i>Erprobungsgruppe</i> 210 mistakenly bombed <a href="/wiki/Croydon_Airport" title="Croydon Airport">Croydon airfield</a> (on the outskirts of London) instead of the intended target, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Kenley" title="RAF Kenley">RAF Kenley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>German intelligence reports made the Luftwaffe optimistic that the RAF, thought to be dependent on local air control, was struggling with supply problems and pilot losses. After a major raid attacking <a href="/wiki/London_Biggin_Hill_Airport#History" title="London Biggin Hill Airport">Biggin Hill</a> on 18 August, Luftwaffe aircrew said they had been unopposed, the airfield was "completely destroyed", and asked "Is England already finished?" In accordance with the strategy agreed on 6 August, defeat of the RAF was to be followed by bombing military and economic targets, systematically extending up to the Midlands.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201381\xe2\x80\x9382_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201381\xe2\x80\x9382-234">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>G\xc3\xb6ring ordered attacks on aircraft factories on 19 August 1940.<sup id="cite_ref-Taylor_and_Mayer_p.74_205-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor_and_Mayer_p.74-205">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> Sixty raids on the night of 19/20 August targeted the aircraft industry and harbours, and bombs fell on suburban areas around London: <a href="/wiki/Croydon" title="Croydon">Croydon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wimbledon,_London" title="Wimbledon, London">Wimbledon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Municipal_Borough_of_Malden_and_Coombe" title="Municipal Borough of Malden and Coombe">the Maldens</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201382_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201382-235">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup>\nNight raids were made on 21/22 August on <a href="/wiki/Aberdeen" title="Aberdeen">Aberdeen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Wales" title="South Wales">South Wales</a>. That morning, bombs were dropped on <a href="/wiki/Harrow,_London" title="Harrow, London">Harrow</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wealdstone" title="Wealdstone">Wealdstone</a>, on the outskirts of London. Overnight on 22/23 August, the output of an aircraft factory at <a href="/wiki/Filton" title="Filton">Filton</a> near Bristol was drastically affected by a raid in which Ju88 bombers released over 16 tons of high explosive bombs.\nOn the night of 23/24 August over 200 bombers attacked the <a href="/wiki/Fort_Dunlop" title="Fort Dunlop">Fort Dunlop</a> tyre factory in <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a>, with a significant effect on production. A sustained bombing campaign began on 24 August with the largest raid so far, killing 100 in <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth" title="Portsmouth">Portsmouth</a>, and that night, several areas of London were bombed; the <a href="/wiki/East_End_of_London" title="East End of London">East End</a> was set ablaze and bombs landed on central London. Some historians believe that these bombs were dropped accidentally by a group of <a href="/wiki/Heinkel_He_111" title="Heinkel He 111">Heinkel He 111s</a> which had failed to find their target; this account has been contested.<sup id="cite_ref-19-24aug_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19-24aug-236">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>More night raids were made around London on 24/25 August, when bombs fell on Croydon, <a href="/wiki/Banstead" title="Banstead">Banstead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lewisham" title="Lewisham">Lewisham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uxbridge" title="Uxbridge">Uxbridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harrow,_London" title="Harrow, London">Harrow</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hayes,_Hillingdon" title="Hayes, Hillingdon">Hayes</a>. London was on red alert over the night of 28/29 August, with bombs reported in <a href="/wiki/Finchley" title="Finchley">Finchley</a>, <a href="/wiki/St_Pancras,_London" title="St Pancras, London">St Pancras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wembley" title="Wembley">Wembley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wood_Green" title="Wood Green">Wood Green</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southgate,_London" title="Southgate, London">Southgate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_Kent_Road" title="Old Kent Road">Old Kent Road</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mill_Hill" title="Mill Hill">Mill Hill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ilford" title="Ilford">Ilford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chigwell" title="Chigwell">Chigwell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hendon" title="Hendon">Hendon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201382\xe2\x80\x9383_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201382\xe2\x80\x9383-147">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Attacks_on_airfields_from_24_August">Attacks on airfields from 24 August</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Attacks on airfields from 24 August">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pilots_of_No._303_(Polish)_Squadron_RAF_with_one_of_their_Hawker_Hurricanes,_October_1940._CH1535.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Pilots_of_No._303_%28Polish%29_Squadron_RAF_with_one_of_their_Hawker_Hurricanes%2C_October_1940._CH1535.jpg/220px-Pilots_of_No._303_%28Polish%29_Squadron_RAF_with_one_of_their_Hawker_Hurricanes%2C_October_1940._CH1535.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Pilots_of_No._303_%28Polish%29_Squadron_RAF_with_one_of_their_Hawker_Hurricanes%2C_October_1940._CH1535.jpg/330px-Pilots_of_No._303_%28Polish%29_Squadron_RAF_with_one_of_their_Hawker_Hurricanes%2C_October_1940._CH1535.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Pilots_of_No._303_%28Polish%29_Squadron_RAF_with_one_of_their_Hawker_Hurricanes%2C_October_1940._CH1535.jpg/440px-Pilots_of_No._303_%28Polish%29_Squadron_RAF_with_one_of_their_Hawker_Hurricanes%2C_October_1940._CH1535.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1278" data-file-height="956" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pilots_of_No._303_(Polish)_Squadron_RAF_with_one_of_their_Hawker_Hurricanes,_October_1940._CH1535.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Polish <a href="/wiki/303_squadron" class="mw-redirect" title="303 squadron">303 squadron</a> pilots, 1940. Left to right: P/O <a href="/wiki/Miros%C5%82aw_Feri%C4%87" title="Miros\xc5\x82aw Feri\xc4\x87">Feri\xc4\x87</a>, Flt Lt <a href="/wiki/John_A._Kent" title="John A. Kent">Kent</a>, F/O Grzeszczak, P/O Radomski, P/O <a href="/wiki/Jan_Zumbach" title="Jan Zumbach">Zumbach</a>, P/O <a href="/wiki/Witold_%C5%81okuciewski" title="Witold \xc5\x81okuciewski">\xc5\x81okuciewski</a>, F/O <a href="/wiki/Zdzis%C5%82aw_Henneberg" title="Zdzis\xc5\x82aw Henneberg">Henneberg</a>, Sgt. Rogowski, Sgt. Szaposznikow.</div></div></div>\n<p>G\xc3\xb6ring\'s directive issued on 23 August 1940 ordered ceaseless attacks on the aircraft industry and on RAF ground organisation to force the RAF to use its fighters, continuing the tactic of luring them up to be destroyed, and added that focussed attacks were to be made on RAF airfields.<sup id="cite_ref-19-24aug_236-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19-24aug-236">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From 24 August onwards, the battle was a fight between Kesselring\'s <i>Luftflotte</i> 2 and Park\'s 11 Group. The Luftwaffe concentrated all their strength on knocking out Fighter Command and made repeated attacks on the airfields. Of the 33 heavy attacks in the following two weeks, 24 were against airfields. The key sector stations were hit repeatedly: <a href="/wiki/RAF_Biggin_Hill" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF Biggin Hill">Biggin Hill</a> and <a href="/wiki/RAF_Hornchurch" title="RAF Hornchurch">Hornchurch</a> four times each; <a href="/wiki/RAF_Debden" title="RAF Debden">Debden</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Weald_airfield" class="mw-redirect" title="North Weald airfield">North Weald</a> twice each. <a href="/wiki/Croydon_Airport" title="Croydon Airport">Croydon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gravesend_Airport" title="Gravesend Airport">Gravesend</a>, <a href="/wiki/London_Southend_Airport" title="London Southend Airport">Rochford</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Hawkinge" title="RAF Hawkinge">Hawkinge</a> and <a href="/wiki/RAF_Manston" title="RAF Manston">Manston</a> were also attacked in strength. <a href="/wiki/Coastal_Command" class="mw-redirect" title="Coastal Command">Coastal Command</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/RAF_Eastchurch" title="RAF Eastchurch">Eastchurch</a> was bombed at least seven times because it was believed to be a Fighter Command aerodrome. At times these raids caused some damage to the sector stations, threatening the integrity of the Dowding system.\n</p><p>To offset some losses, some 58 Fleet Air Arm fighter pilot volunteers were seconded to RAF squadrons, and a similar number of former Fairey Battle pilots were used. Most replacements from <a href="/wiki/Operational_Conversion_Unit" class="mw-redirect" title="Operational Conversion Unit">Operational Training Units</a> (OTUs) had as little as nine hours flying time and no gunnery or air-to-air combat training. At this point, the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain_Foreign_Contribution" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Britain Foreign Contribution">multinational nature of Fighter Command</a> came to the fore. Many squadrons and personnel from the air forces of the <a href="/wiki/British_Dominions" class="mw-redirect" title="British Dominions">Dominions</a> were already <a href="/wiki/List_of_Royal_Air_Force_aircraft_squadrons" title="List of Royal Air Force aircraft squadrons">attached to the RAF</a>, including top level commanders \xe2\x80\x93 Australians, <a href="/wiki/Canadians" title="Canadians">Canadians</a>, New Zealanders, <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia" title="Rhodesia">Rhodesians</a> and South Africans. In addition, there were other nationalities represented, including <a href="/wiki/Free_French_Forces" class="mw-redirect" title="Free French Forces">Free French</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgian</a> and a Jewish pilot from the <a href="/wiki/Mandatory_Palestine" title="Mandatory Palestine">British mandate of Palestine</a>.\n</p><p>They were bolstered by the arrival of fresh Czechoslovak and <a href="/wiki/Polish_Air_Forces_in_France_and_Great_Britain" title="Polish Air Forces in France and Great Britain">Polish squadrons</a>. These had been held back by Dowding, who mistakenly thought non-English speaking aircrew would have trouble working within his control system: Polish and Czech fliers proved to be especially effective. The pre-war <a href="/wiki/Polish_Air_Force" title="Polish Air Force">Polish Air Force</a> had lengthy and extensive training, and high standards; with Poland conquered and under <a href="/wiki/World_War_II_crimes_in_Poland" class="mw-redirect" title="World War II crimes in Poland">brutal</a> <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Poland_(1939%E2%80%931945)" title="Occupation of Poland (1939\xe2\x80\x931945)">German occupation</a>, the pilots of <a href="/wiki/No._303_Polish_Fighter_Squadron" title="No. 303 Polish Fighter Squadron">No. 303 (Polish) Squadron</a>, the highest-scoring Allied unit,<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> were strongly motivated. <a href="/wiki/Josef_Franti%C5%A1ek" title="Josef Franti\xc5\xa1ek">Josef Franti\xc5\xa1ek</a>, a Czech regular airman who had flown from the occupation of his own country to join <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Poland" title="Invasion of Poland">the Polish</a> and then French air forces before arriving in Britain, flew as a guest of 303 Squadron and was ultimately credited with the highest "RAF score" in the Battle of Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The RAF had the advantage of fighting over home territory. Pilots who bailed out of their downed aircraft could be back at their airfields within hours, while if low on fuel and/or ammunition they could be immediately rearmed.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> One RAF pilot interviewed in late 1940 had been shot down five times during the Battle of Britain, but was able to crash land in Britain or bail out each time.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> For Luftwaffe aircrews, a bailout over England meant capture \xe2\x80\x93 in the critical August period, almost exactly as many Luftwaffe pilots were taken prisoner as were killed<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> \xe2\x80\x93 while parachuting into the English Channel often meant drowning or death from exposure. Morale began to suffer, and <i>[Kanalkrankheit]</i> ("Channel sickness") \xe2\x80\x93 a form of <a href="/wiki/Combat_stress_reaction" title="Combat stress reaction">combat fatigue</a> \xe2\x80\x93 began to appear among the German pilots. Their replacement problem became even worse than the British.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Assessment_of_attempt_to_destroy_the_RAF">Assessment of attempt to destroy the RAF</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Assessment of attempt to destroy the RAF">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The effect of the German attacks on airfields is unclear. According to <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Bungay" title="Stephen Bungay">Stephen Bungay</a>, Dowding, in a letter to <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Trenchard" class="mw-redirect" title="Hugh Trenchard">Hugh Trenchard</a><sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> accompanying Park\'s report on the period 8 August \xe2\x80\x93 10 September 1940, states that the Luftwaffe "achieved very little" in the last week of August and the first week of September.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_2000,_pp._368-369_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_2000,_pp._368-369-243">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> The only Sector Station to be shut down operationally was <a href="/wiki/Biggin_Hill" title="Biggin Hill">Biggin Hill</a>, and it was non-operational for just two hours. Dowding admitted 11 Group\'s efficiency was impaired but, despite serious damage to some airfields, only two out of 13 heavily attacked airfields were down for more than a few hours. The German refocus on London was not critical.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_2000,_pp._368-369_243-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_2000,_pp._368-369-243">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Retired air marshal <a href="/w/index.php?title=Peter_Dye&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Peter Dye (page does not exist)">Peter Dye</a>, head of the RAF Museum, discussed the logistics of the battle in 2000<sup id="cite_ref-Dye00._244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dye00.-244">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> and 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-Dye2010._245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dye2010.-245">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> dealing specifically with the single-seat fighters. Dye contends that not only was British aircraft production replacing aircraft, but replacement pilots were keeping pace with losses. The number of pilots in RAF Fighter Command increased during July, August and September. The figures indicate the number of pilots available never decreased: from July, 1,200 were available, and from 1 August, 1,400 were available. Just over that number were in the field by September. In October the figure was nearly 1,600. By 1 November 1,800 were available. Throughout the battle, the RAF had more fighter pilots available than the Luftwaffe.<sup id="cite_ref-Dye00._244-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dye00.-244">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dye2010._245-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dye2010.-245">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup>  Although the RAF\'s reserves of single seat fighters fell during July, the wastage was made up for by an efficient <a href="/wiki/Civilian_Repair_Organisation" title="Civilian Repair Organisation">Civilian Repair Organisation</a> (CRO), which by December had repaired and put back into service some 4,955 aircraft,<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> and by aircraft held at Air Servicing Unit (ASU) airfields.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RAF_Fighter_Command_1940_HU104508.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/RAF_Fighter_Command_1940_HU104508.jpg/220px-RAF_Fighter_Command_1940_HU104508.jpg" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/RAF_Fighter_Command_1940_HU104508.jpg/330px-RAF_Fighter_Command_1940_HU104508.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/RAF_Fighter_Command_1940_HU104508.jpg/440px-RAF_Fighter_Command_1940_HU104508.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="526" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RAF_Fighter_Command_1940_HU104508.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pilots of <a href="/wiki/No._66_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 66 Squadron RAF">No. 66 Squadron</a> at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Gravesend" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF Gravesend">Gravesend</a>, September 1940</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Richard_Overy" title="Richard Overy">Richard Overy</a> agrees with Dye and Bungay. Overy asserts only one airfield was temporarily put out of action and "only" 103 pilots were lost. British fighter production produced 496 new aircraft in July and 467 in August, and another 467 in September (not counting repaired aircraft), covering the losses of August and September. Overy indicates the number of serviceable and total strength returns reveal an <i>increase</i> in fighters from 3 August to 7 September, 1,061 on strength and 708 serviceable to 1,161 on strength and 746 serviceable.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> Moreover, Overy points out that the number of RAF fighter pilots grew by one-third between June and August 1940. Personnel records show a constant supply of around 1,400 pilots in the crucial weeks of the battle. In the second half of September it reached 1,500. The shortfall of pilots was never above 10%. The Germans never had more than between 1,100 and 1,200 pilots, a deficiency of up to one-third. "If Fighter Command were \'the few\', the German fighter pilots were fewer".<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Other scholars assert that this period was the most dangerous of all. In <i>The Narrow Margin</i>, published in 1961, historians <a href="/wiki/Derek_Wood_(author)" title="Derek Wood (author)">Derek Wood</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Derek_Dempster&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Derek Dempster (page does not exist)">Derek Dempster</a> believed that the two weeks from 24 August to 6 September represented a real danger. According to them, from 24 August to 6 September 295 fighters had been totally destroyed and 171 badly damaged, against a total output of 269 new and repaired Spitfires and Hurricanes. They assert that 103 pilots were killed or missing and 128 were wounded, which represented a total wastage of 120 pilots per week out of a fighting strength of just fewer than 1,000. They conclude that during August no more than 260 fighter pilots were turned out by OTUs and casualties in the same month were just over 300. A full squadron establishment was 26 pilots whereas the average in August was 16. In their assessment, the RAF was losing the battle.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Denis_Richards" title="Denis Richards">Denis Richards</a>, in his 1953 contribution to the official British account <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Second_World_War" title="History of the Second World War">History of the Second World War</a></i>, agreed that lack of pilots, especially experienced ones, was the RAF\'s greatest problem. He states that between 8 and 18 August 154 RAF pilots were killed, severely wounded, or missing, while only 63 new pilots were trained. Availability of aircraft was also a serious issue. While its reserves during the Battle of Britain never declined to a half dozen planes as some later claimed, Richards describes 24 August to 6 September as the critical period because during these two weeks Germany destroyed far more aircraft through its attacks on 11 Group\'s southeast bases than Britain was producing. Three more weeks of such a pace would indeed have exhausted aircraft reserves. Germany had seen heavy losses of pilots and aircraft as well, thus its shift to night-time attacks in September. On 7 September RAF aircraft losses fell below British production and remained so until the end of the war.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Day_and_night_attacks_on_London:_start_of_the_Blitz">Day and night attacks on London: start of the Blitz</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Day and night attacks on London: start of the Blitz">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/The_Blitz" title="The Blitz">The Blitz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain_Day" title="Battle of Britain Day">Battle of Britain Day</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Goering_giving_a_speech_to_his_fighter_pilots_near_Calais_September_1940.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5e/Goering_giving_a_speech_to_his_fighter_pilots_near_Calais_September_1940.jpg/330px-Goering_giving_a_speech_to_his_fighter_pilots_near_Calais_September_1940.jpg" width="330" height="119" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5e/Goering_giving_a_speech_to_his_fighter_pilots_near_Calais_September_1940.jpg/495px-Goering_giving_a_speech_to_his_fighter_pilots_near_Calais_September_1940.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/5/5e/Goering_giving_a_speech_to_his_fighter_pilots_near_Calais_September_1940.jpg 2x" data-file-width="525" data-file-height="190" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Goering_giving_a_speech_to_his_fighter_pilots_near_Calais_September_1940.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Calais, September 1940. G\xc3\xb6ring giving a speech to pilots about the change in tactics: to bomb London instead of the airfields</div></div></div>\n<p>Hitler\'s "Directive No. 17 \xe2\x80\x93 For the conduct of air and sea warfare against England" issued on 1 August 1940, reserved to himself the right to decide on terror attacks as measures of reprisal.<sup id="cite_ref-Dir_17_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dir_17-68">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\nHitler issued a directive that London was not to be bombed save on his sole instruction.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> In preparation, detailed target plans under the code name <a href="/wiki/The_Blitz#Loge_and_Seeschlange" title="The Blitz">Operation <i>Loge</i></a> for raids on communications, power stations, armaments works and docks in the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_London" title="Port of London">Port of London</a> were distributed to the <i>Fliegerkorps</i> in July. The port areas were crowded next to residential housing and civilian casualties would be expected, but this would combine military and economic targets with indirect effects on morale. The strategy agreed on 6 August was for raids on military and economic targets in towns and cities to culminate in a major attack on London.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201384\xe2\x80\x9385_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201384\xe2\x80\x9385-253">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup>\nIn mid August raids were made on targets on the outskirts of London.<sup id="cite_ref-19-24aug_236-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19-24aug-236">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Luftwaffe doctrine included the possibility of retaliatory attacks on cities, and since 11 May small scale night raids by <a href="/wiki/RAF_Bomber_Command" title="RAF Bomber Command">RAF Bomber Command</a> had frequently bombed residential areas. The Germans assumed this was deliberate, and as the raids increased in frequency and scale the population grew impatient for measures of revenge.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201384\xe2\x80\x9385_253-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201384\xe2\x80\x9385-253">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup>\nOn 25 August 1940, 81 bombers of Bomber Command were sent out to raid industrial and commercial targets in Berlin. Clouds prevented accurate identification and the bombs fell across the city, causing some casualties among the civilian population as well as damage to residential areas.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> Continuing RAF raids on Berlin led to Hitler withdrawing his directive on 30 August,<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> and giving the go-ahead to the planned bombing offensive.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201384\xe2\x80\x9385_253-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201384\xe2\x80\x9385-253">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> On 3 September G\xc3\xb6ring planned to bomb London daily, with General Albert <a href="/wiki/Kesselring" title="Kesselring">Kesselring</a>\'s enthusiastic support, having received reports the average strength of RAF squadrons was down to five or seven fighters out of twelve and their airfields in the area were out of action. Hitler issued a directive on 5 September to attack cities including London.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup>  In his widely publicised speech delivered on 4 September 1940, Hitler condemned the bombing of Berlin and presented the planned attacks on London as reprisals. The first daylight raid was titled <i>Vergeltungsangriff</i> (revenge attack).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201383_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201383-258">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:London_Blitz_791940.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/London_Blitz_791940.jpg/220px-London_Blitz_791940.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/London_Blitz_791940.jpg/330px-London_Blitz_791940.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/London_Blitz_791940.jpg/440px-London_Blitz_791940.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2940" data-file-height="1916" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:London_Blitz_791940.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Smoke rising from fires in the London docks, following bombing on 7 September</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Heinkel_over_Wapping.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Heinkel_over_Wapping.jpg/220px-Heinkel_over_Wapping.jpg" width="220" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Heinkel_over_Wapping.jpg/330px-Heinkel_over_Wapping.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Heinkel_over_Wapping.jpg/440px-Heinkel_over_Wapping.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4472" data-file-height="4420" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Heinkel_over_Wapping.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Heinkel_He_111" title="Heinkel He 111">Heinkel He 111</a> bomber over the <a href="/wiki/Surrey_Commercial_Docks" title="Surrey Commercial Docks">Surrey Commercial Docks</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_London" title="South London">South London</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wapping" title="Wapping">Wapping</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Dogs" title="Isle of Dogs">Isle of Dogs</a> in the <a href="/wiki/East_End_of_London" title="East End of London">East End of London</a> on 7 September 1940</div></div></div>\n<p>On 7 September, a massive series of raids involving nearly four hundred bombers and more than six hundred fighters targeted docks in the East End of London, day and night. The RAF anticipated attacks on airfields and 11 Group rose to meet them, in greater numbers than the Luftwaffe expected. The first official deployment of 12 Group\'s Leigh-Mallory\'s <a href="/wiki/Big_Wing" title="Big Wing">Big Wing</a> took twenty minutes to form up, missing its intended target, but encountering another formation of bombers while still climbing. They returned, apologetic about their limited success, and blamed the delay on being scrambled too late.<sup id="cite_ref-7sept_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7sept-259">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Air_Raid_Shelter_Under_the_Railway_Arches,_South_East_London,_England,_1940_D1587.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Air_Raid_Shelter_Under_the_Railway_Arches%2C_South_East_London%2C_England%2C_1940_D1587.jpg/220px-Air_Raid_Shelter_Under_the_Railway_Arches%2C_South_East_London%2C_England%2C_1940_D1587.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Air_Raid_Shelter_Under_the_Railway_Arches%2C_South_East_London%2C_England%2C_1940_D1587.jpg/330px-Air_Raid_Shelter_Under_the_Railway_Arches%2C_South_East_London%2C_England%2C_1940_D1587.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Air_Raid_Shelter_Under_the_Railway_Arches%2C_South_East_London%2C_England%2C_1940_D1587.jpg/440px-Air_Raid_Shelter_Under_the_Railway_Arches%2C_South_East_London%2C_England%2C_1940_D1587.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="522" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Air_Raid_Shelter_Under_the_Railway_Arches,_South_East_London,_England,_1940_D1587.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Air-raid shelter in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, 1940</div></div></div>\n<p>The German press jubilantly announced that "one great cloud of smoke stretches tonight from the middle of London to the mouth of the Thames." Reports reflected the briefings given to crews before the raids \xe2\x80\x93 "Everyone knew about the last cowardly attacks on German cities, and thought about wives, mothers and children. And then came that word \'Vengeance!\'" Pilots reported seeing ruined airfields as they flew towards London, appearances which gave intelligence reports the impression of devastated defences. G\xc3\xb6ring maintained that the RAF was close to defeat, making invasion feasible.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201383,_87_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201383,_87-261">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fighter Command had been at its lowest ebb, short of men and machines, and the break from airfield attacks allowed them to recover. 11 Group had considerable success in breaking up daytime raids. 12 Group repeatedly disobeyed orders and failed to meet requests to protect 11 Group airfields, but their experiments with increasingly large Big Wings had some success. The Luftwaffe began to abandon their morning raids, with attacks on London starting late in the afternoon for fifty-seven consecutive nights.<sup id="cite_ref-8sept_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8sept-262">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_britain_firefighting.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Battle_of_britain_firefighting.jpg/220px-Battle_of_britain_firefighting.jpg" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Battle_of_britain_firefighting.jpg/330px-Battle_of_britain_firefighting.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Battle_of_britain_firefighting.jpg/440px-Battle_of_britain_firefighting.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1411" data-file-height="1087" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_britain_firefighting.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Members of the London Auxiliary Firefighting Service</div></div></div>\n<p>The most damaging aspect to the Luftwaffe of targeting London was the increase in range. The Bf 109E escorts had a limited fuel capacity resulting in only a 660&#160;km (410 mile) maximum range solely on internal fuel,<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> and when they arrived had only 10 minutes of flying time before turning for home, leaving the bombers undefended by fighter escorts. Its eventual stablemate, the <a href="/wiki/Focke-Wulf_Fw_190" title="Focke-Wulf Fw 190">Focke-Wulf Fw 190</a>A, was flying only in prototype form in mid-1940; the first 28 Fw 190s were not delivered until November 1940. The Fw 190A-1 had a maximum range of 940&#160;km (584 miles) on internal fuel, 40% greater than the Bf 109E.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Messerschmitt_Bf_109" title="Messerschmitt Bf 109">Messerschmitt Bf 109</a>E-7 corrected this deficiency by adding a ventral centre-line ordnance rack to take either an SC 250 bomb or a standard 300 litre Luftwaffe <a href="/wiki/Drop_tank" title="Drop tank">drop tank</a> to double the range to 1,325&#160;km (820&#160;mi). The ordnance rack was not retrofitted to earlier Bf 109Es until October 1940.\n</p><p>On 14 September, Hitler chaired a meeting with the OKW staff. G\xc3\xb6ring was in France directing the decisive battle, so <a href="/wiki/Erhard_Milch" title="Erhard Milch">Erhard Milch</a>  deputised for him.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> Hitler asked "Should we call it off altogether?" General Hans <a href="/wiki/Jeschonnek" class="mw-redirect" title="Jeschonnek">Jeschonnek</a>, Luftwaffe Chief of Staff, begged for a last chance to defeat the RAF and for permission to launch attacks on civilian residential areas to cause mass panic. Hitler refused the latter, perhaps unaware of how much damage had already been done to civilian targets. He reserved for himself the power to unleash the terror weapon. Instead political will was to be broken by destroying the material infrastructure, the weapons industry, and stocks of fuel and food.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dorniervictoriastation.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ac/Dorniervictoriastation.jpg/290px-Dorniervictoriastation.jpg" width="290" height="105" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ac/Dorniervictoriastation.jpg/435px-Dorniervictoriastation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ac/Dorniervictoriastation.jpg/580px-Dorniervictoriastation.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="289" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dorniervictoriastation.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Zehbe\'s Dornier falling on Victoria Station after being rammed by <a href="/wiki/Ray_Holmes" title="Ray Holmes">Ray Holmes</a>, 15 September 1940</div></div></div>\n<p>On 15 September, two massive waves of German attacks were decisively repulsed by the RAF by deploying every aircraft in 11 Group. Sixty German and twenty-six RAF aircraft were shot down. The action was the climax of the Battle of Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Two days after the German defeat Hitler postponed preparations for the invasion of Britain. Henceforth, in the face of mounting losses in men, aircraft and the lack of adequate replacements, the Luftwaffe completed their gradual shift from daylight bomber raids and continued with nighttime bombing. 15 September is commemorated as <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain_Day" title="Battle of Britain Day">Battle of Britain Day</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Night_time_Blitz.2C_fighter-bomber_day_raids"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Night_time_Blitz,_fighter-bomber_day_raids">Night time Blitz, fighter-bomber day raids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Night time Blitz, fighter-bomber day raids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Blitz" title="The Blitz">The Blitz</a></div>\n<p>At the 14 September OKW conference, Hitler acknowledged that the Luftwaffe had still not gained the air superiority needed for the <a href="/wiki/Operation_Sea_Lion" title="Operation Sea Lion">Operation Sealion</a> invasion. In agreement with <a href="/wiki/Erich_Raeder" title="Erich Raeder">Raeder</a>\'s written recommendation, Hitler said the campaign was to intensify regardless of invasion plans: "The decisive thing is the ceaseless continuation of air attacks." <a href="/wiki/Hans_Jeschonnek" title="Hans Jeschonnek">Jeschonnek</a> proposed attacking residential areas to cause "mass panic", but Hitler turned this down: he reserved to himself the option of terror bombing. British morale was to be broken by destroying infrastructure, armaments manufacturing, fuel and food stocks. On 16 September, G\xc3\xb6ring gave the order for this change in strategy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery200187\xe2\x80\x9389_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery200187\xe2\x80\x9389-69">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> This new phase was to be the first independent <a href="/wiki/Strategic_bombing" title="Strategic bombing">strategic bombing</a> campaign, in hopes of a political success forcing the British to give up.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201390_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201390-70">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\nHitler hoped it might result in "eight million going mad" (referring to the population of London in 1940), which would "cause a catastrophe" for the British. In those circumstances, Hitler said, "even a small invasion might go a long way". Hitler was against cancelling the invasion as "the cancellation would reach the ears of the enemy and strengthen his resolve".<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;nb 18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;nb 19&#93;</a></sup> On 19 September, Hitler ordered a reduction in work on Sealion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery200188_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery200188-270">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> He doubted if strategic bombing could achieve its aims, but ending the air war would be an open admission of defeat. He had to maintain the appearance of concentration on defeating Britain, to conceal from <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a> his covert aim to <a href="/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa" title="Operation Barbarossa">invade the Soviet Union</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201391_271-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201391-271">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Spitfires_camera_gun_film_shows_tracer_ammunition.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Spitfires_camera_gun_film_shows_tracer_ammunition.jpg/220px-Spitfires_camera_gun_film_shows_tracer_ammunition.jpg" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Spitfires_camera_gun_film_shows_tracer_ammunition.jpg/330px-Spitfires_camera_gun_film_shows_tracer_ammunition.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Spitfires_camera_gun_film_shows_tracer_ammunition.jpg/440px-Spitfires_camera_gun_film_shows_tracer_ammunition.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="597" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Spitfires_camera_gun_film_shows_tracer_ammunition.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gun_camera" title="Gun camera">Gun camera</a> film shows tracer ammunition from a Supermarine Spitfire Mark I of <a href="/wiki/No._609_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 609 Squadron RAF">609 Squadron</a>, flown by <a href="/wiki/Flight_Lieutenant" class="mw-redirect" title="Flight Lieutenant">Flight Lieutenant</a> J H G McArthur, hitting a <a href="/wiki/Heinkel_He_111" title="Heinkel He 111">Heinkel He 111</a> on its starboard quarter. These aircraft were part of a large formation from KG 53 and 55 which attacked the Bristol Aeroplane Company\'s works at Filton, Bristol, just before midday on 25 September 1940.</div></div></div>\n<p>Throughout the battle, most Luftwaffe bombing raids had been at night.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201371_272-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201371-272">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> They increasingly suffered unsustainable losses in daylight raids, and the last massive daytime attacks were on 15 September. A raid of 70 bombers on 18 September also suffered badly, and day raids were gradually phased out leaving the main attacks at night. Fighter command still lacked any successful way of intercepting night-time raiders, the <a href="/wiki/Night_fighter" title="Night fighter">night fighter</a> force was mostly <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Blenheim" title="Bristol Blenheim">Blenheims</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Beaufighter" title="Bristol Beaufighter">Beaufighters</a>, and lacked airborne radar so had no way of finding the bombers. <a href="/wiki/Anti-aircraft_warfare" title="Anti-aircraft warfare">Anti-aircraft guns</a> were diverted to London\'s defences, but had a much reduced success rate against night attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery200178\xe2\x80\x9389,_95\xe2\x80\x9396_273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery200178\xe2\x80\x9389,_95\xe2\x80\x9396-273">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From mid September, Luftwaffe daylight bombing was gradually taken over by a <a href="/wiki/Messerschmitt_Bf_109_variants" title="Messerschmitt Bf 109 variants">Bf 109 fighters</a>, adapted to take one 250&#160;kg bomb. Small groups of fighter-bombers would carry out <i>St\xc3\xb6rangriffe</i> raids escorted by large escort formations of about 200 to 300 combat fighters. They flew at altitudes over 20,000 feet (6,100&#160;m) where the Bf109 had an advantage over RAF fighters, except the Spitfire.<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">&#91;nb 20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">&#91;nb 21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> The raids disturbed civilians, and continued the war of attrition against Fighter Command. The raids were intended to carry out precision bombing on military or economic targets, but it was hard to achieve sufficient accuracy with the single bomb. Sometimes, when attacked, the fighter-bombers had to jettison the bomb to function as fighters. The RAF was at a disadvantage, and changed defensive tactics by introducing standing patrols of Spitfires at high altitude to monitor incoming raids. On a sighting, other patrols at lower altitude would fly up to join the battle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery200195\xe2\x80\x9397_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery200195\xe2\x80\x9397-279">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201391_271-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201391-271">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A Junkers Ju 88 returning from a raid on London was shot down in <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a> on 27 September resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Graveney_Marsh" title="Battle of Graveney Marsh">Battle of Graveney Marsh</a>, the last action between British and foreign military forces on British mainland soil.<sup id="cite_ref-KO_280-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KO-280">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>German bombing of Britain reached its peak in October and November 1940. In post war interrogation, <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Keitel" title="Wilhelm Keitel">Wilhelm Keitel</a> described the aims as economic blockade, in conjunction with <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Atlantic" title="Battle of the Atlantic">submarine warfare</a>, and attrition of Britain\'s military and economic resources. The Luftwaffe wanted to achieve victory on its own, and was reluctant to cooperate with the navy. Their strategy for blockade was to destroy ports and storage facilities in towns and cities. Priorities were based on the pattern of trade and distribution, so for these months London was the main target.
