In November their attention turned to other ports and industrial targets around Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery201390\xe2\x80\x9393_281-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery201390\xe2\x80\x9393-281">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Hitler postponed the Sealion invasion on 13 October "until the spring of 1941". It was not until Hitler\'s Directive 21 was issued, on 18 December 1940, that the threat to Britain of invasion finally ended.<sup id="cite_ref-Taylor_and_Mayer_p.74_205-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor_and_Mayer_p.74-205">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Royal_family">Royal family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Royal family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>During the battle, and for the rest of the war, an important factor in keeping public morale high was the continued presence in London of <a href="/wiki/George_VI" title="George VI">King George VI</a> and his wife <a href="/wiki/Queen_Elizabeth_The_Queen_Mother" title="Queen Elizabeth The Queen Mother">Queen Elizabeth</a>. When war broke out in 1939, the King and Queen decided to stay in London and not flee to Canada, as had been suggested.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">&#91;nb 22&#93;</a></sup> George VI and Elizabeth officially stayed in <a href="/wiki/Buckingham_Palace" title="Buckingham Palace">Buckingham Palace</a> throughout the war, although they often spent weekends at <a href="/wiki/Windsor_Castle" title="Windsor Castle">Windsor Castle</a> to visit their daughters, Elizabeth (the future <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">queen</a>) and Margaret.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> Buckingham Palace was damaged by bombs which landed in the grounds on 10 September and, on 13 September, more serious damage was caused by two bombs which destroyed the Royal Chapel. The royal couple were in a small sitting room about 80 yards from where the bombs exploded.<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> On 24 September, in recognition of the bravery of civilians, King George VI inaugurated the award of the <a href="/wiki/George_Cross" title="George Cross">George Cross</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Attrition_statistics">Attrition statistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Attrition statistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Confirmation_and_overclaiming_of_aerial_victories" class="mw-redirect" title="Confirmation and overclaiming of aerial victories">Confirmation and overclaiming of aerial victories</a></div>\n<p>Overall, by 2 November, the RAF fielded 1,796 pilots, an increase of over 40% from July 1940\'s count of 1,259 pilots.<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup> Based on German sources (from a Luftwaffe intelligence officer <a href="/w/index.php?title=Otto_Bechtle&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Otto Bechtle (page does not exist)">Otto Bechtle</a> attached to <a href="/wiki/KG_2" class="mw-redirect" title="KG 2">KG 2</a> in February 1944) translated by the <a href="/wiki/Air_Historical_Branch" title="Air Historical Branch">Air Historical Branch</a>, Stephen Bungay asserts German fighter and bomber "strength" declined without recovery, and that from August\xe2\x80\x93December 1940, the German fighter and bomber strength declined by 30 and 25 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_p._368_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_p._368-15">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> In contrast, Williamson Murray, argues (using translations by the Air Historical Branch) that 1,380 German bombers were on strength on 29 June 1940,<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_p._107_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_p._107-10">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Murray_1983,_p._53_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murray_1983,_p._53-287">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> 1,420 bombers on 28 September,<sup id="cite_ref-Murray_1983,_p._56_288-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murray_1983,_p._56-288">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup> 1,423 level bombers on 2 November<sup id="cite_ref-Murray_1983,_p._55_289-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murray_1983,_p._55-289">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> and 1,393 bombers on 30 November 1940.<sup id="cite_ref-Murray_1983,_p._55_289-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murray_1983,_p._55-289">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> In July\xe2\x80\x93September the number of Luftwaffe pilots available fell by 136, but the number of operational pilots had shrunk by 171 by September. The training organisation of the Luftwaffe was failing to replace losses. German fighter pilots, in contrast to popular perception, were not afforded training or rest rotations unlike their British counterparts.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_2000,_p._370_128-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_2000,_p._370-128">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> The first week of September accounted for 25% of Fighter Command\'s and 24% of the <i>Luftwaffe\'s</i> overall losses.<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> Between the dates 26 August \xe2\x80\x93 6 September, on only one day (1 September) did the Germans destroy more aircraft than they lost. Losses were 325 German and 248 British.<sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Luftwaffe losses for August numbered 774 aircraft to all causes, representing 18.5% of all combat aircraft at the beginning of the month.<sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> Fighter Command\'s losses in August were 426 fighters destroyed,<sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup> amounting to 40 per cent of 1,061 fighters available on 3 August.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood_and_Dempster_2003,_p._306_294-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood_and_Dempster_2003,_p._306-294">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> In addition, 99 German bombers and 27 other types were destroyed between 1 and 29 August.<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From July to September, the Luftwaffe\'s loss records indicate the loss of 1,636 aircraft, 1,184 to enemy action.<sup id="cite_ref-Murray_1983,_p._53_287-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murray_1983,_p._53-287">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> This represented 47% of the initial strength of single-engined fighters, 66% of twin-engined fighters, and 45% of bombers. This indicates the Germans were running out of aircrew as well as aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-Murray_1983,_p._52_296-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murray_1983,_p._52-296">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Throughout the battle, the Germans greatly underestimated the size of the RAF and the scale of British aircraft production. Across the Channel, the Air Intelligence division of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=British_Imperial_Air_Ministry&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="British Imperial Air Ministry (page does not exist)">Air Ministry</a> consistently overestimated the size of the German air enemy and the productive capacity of the German aviation industry. As the battle was fought, both sides exaggerated the losses inflicted on the other by an equally large margin. The intelligence picture formed before the battle encouraged the Luftwaffe to believe that such losses pushed Fighter Command to the very edge of defeat, while the exaggerated picture of German air strength persuaded the RAF that the threat it faced was larger and more dangerous than was the case.<sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> This led the British to the conclusion that another fortnight of attacks on airfields might force Fighter Command to withdraw their squadrons from the south of England. The German misconception, on the other hand, encouraged first complacency, then strategic misjudgement. The shift of targets from air bases to industry and communications was taken because it was assumed that Fighter Command was virtually eliminated.<sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Between 24 August and 4 September, German serviceability rates, which were acceptable at <i>Stuka</i> units, were running at 75% with Bf 109s, 70% with bombers and 65% with Bf 110s, indicating a shortage of spare parts. All units were well below established strength. The attrition was beginning to affect the fighters in particular."<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> By 14 September, the Luftwaffe\'s Bf 109 <i>Geschwader</i> possessed only 67% of their operational crews against authorised aircraft. For Bf 110 units it was 46 per cent; and for bombers it was 59 per cent. A week later the figures had dropped to 64 per cent, 52% and 52 per cent.<sup id="cite_ref-Murray_1983,_p._52_296-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murray_1983,_p._52-296">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> Serviceability rates in Fighter Command\'s fighter squadrons, between 24 August and 7 September, were listed as: 64.8% on 24 August; 64.7% on 31 August and 64.25% on 7 September 1940.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood_and_Dempster_2003,_p._306_294-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood_and_Dempster_2003,_p._306-294">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Due to the failure of the Luftwaffe to establish air supremacy, a conference assembled on 14 September at Hitler\'s headquarters. Hitler concluded that air superiority had not yet been established and "promised to review the situation on 17 September for possible landings on 27 September or 8 October. Three days later, when the evidence was clear that the German Air Force had greatly exaggerated the extent of their successes against the RAF, Hitler postponed <i>Sea Lion</i> indefinitely."<sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Propaganda">Propaganda</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Propaganda">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Propaganda was an important element of the air war which began to develop over Britain from 18 June 1940 onwards, when the <i>Luftwaffe</i> began small, probing daylight raids to test RAF defences. One of many examples of these small-scale raids was the destruction of a school at Polruan in Cornwall, by a single raider. Into early July, the British media\'s focus on the air battles increased steadily, the press, magazines, BBC radio and newsreels daily conveying the contents of Air Ministry communiques.<sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup> The German <a href="/wiki/Wehrmachtbericht" title="Wehrmachtbericht">OKW communiques</a> matched Britain\'s efforts in claiming for the upper hand.<sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Central to the propaganda war on both sides of the Channel were aircraft claims, this discussed under \'Attrition statistics\'. These daily claims were important both for sustaining British home front morale and persuading America to support Britain, and were produced by the Air Ministry\'s Air Intelligence branch. Under pressure from American journalists and broadcasters to prove that the RAF\'s claims were genuine, RAF intelligence compared pilots\' claims with actual aircraft wrecks and those seen to crash into the sea. It was soon realised that there was a discrepancy between the two, but the Air Ministry decided not to reveal this.<sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup> In fact, it was not until May 1947 that the actual figures were released to the public, by which time it was of far less importance. Many though refused to believe the revised figures, including Douglas Bader.<sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The place of the Battle of Britain in British popular memory is due in no small part to the successful propaganda campaign waged by the Air Ministry, between July\xe2\x80\x93October 1940, but also in valorising the Few from March 1941 onwards. The publication of the immensely successful 3d pamphlet, <i>The Battle of Britain</i>, saw huge international sales, leading even Goebbels to admire its propaganda value. Focusing only upon the Few, with no mention of RAF bomber attacks against invasion barges, the Battle of Britain was soon established as a major victory for Fighter Command. This in turn inspired a wide range of feature films, books, magazines, works of art, poetry, radio plays and MOI short films.\n</p><p>It is notable that this most impressive of British victories had, in essence, been proclaimed within only five months of the cessation of large-scale daylight air battles, and without reference to Hitler and the OKW\'s reasoning for not proceeding with Operation <i>Sea Lion</i>. The continuing post-war popularity of the Battle of Britain is in fact directly attributable to the Air Ministry\'s latter-1940 air communiques, the media in turn broadcasting or publishing RAF aircraft claims. Noted above, this in turn led to the March 1941 pamphlet, which inspired a wide range of cultural responses to the Few, and the Battle of Britain. The Air Ministry built upon this with the development of the Battle of Britain Sunday commemoration, also supported the Battle of Britain clasp for issue to the Few in 1945, and from 1945 Battle of Britain Week. The Battle of Britain window in Westminster Abbey was also encouraged by the Air Ministry, Lords Trenchard and Dowding on its committee. By July 1947 when the window was unveiled, the Battle of Britain had already attained central prominence as Fighter Command\'s most notable victory, the Few alone credited with preventing invasion in 1940. Although given widespread media coverage in September and October 1940, RAF Bomber and Coastal Command raids against invasion barge concentrations had already been forgotten by war\'s end.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath">Aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The Battle of Britain marked the first major defeat of Germany\'s military forces, with air superiority seen as the key to victory.<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup> Pre-war theories had led to exaggerated fears of <a href="/wiki/Strategic_bombing" title="Strategic bombing">strategic bombing</a>, and UK public opinion was buoyed by coming through the ordeal.<sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> For the RAF, Fighter Command had achieved a great victory in successfully carrying out <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Inskip,_1st_Viscount_Caldecote" title="Thomas Inskip, 1st Viscount Caldecote">Sir Thomas Inskip\'s</a> 1937 air policy of preventing the Germans from knocking Britain out of the war. \n</p><p>The battle also significantly shifted American opinion. During the battle, many Americans accepted the view promoted by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Kennedy" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph Kennedy">Joseph Kennedy</a>, the American ambassador in London, who believed that the United Kingdom could not survive. Roosevelt wanted a second opinion, and sent <a href="/wiki/William_Joseph_Donovan" class="mw-redirect" title="William Joseph Donovan">"Wild Bill" Donovan</a> on a brief visit to the UK; he became convinced the UK would survive and should be supported in every possible way.<sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup> Before the end of the year American journalist <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Ingersoll_(PM_publisher)" title="Ralph Ingersoll (PM publisher)">Ralph Ingersoll</a>, after returning from Britain, published a book concluding that "Adolf Hitler met his first defeat in eight years" in what might "go down in history as a battle as important as <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Waterloo" title="Battle of Waterloo">Waterloo</a> or <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gettysburg" title="Battle of Gettysburg">Gettysburg</a>". The turning point was when the Germans reduced the intensity of the Blitz after 15 September. According to Ingersoll, "[a] majority of responsible British officers who fought through this battle believe that if Hitler and G\xc3\xb6ring had had the courage and the resources to lose 200 planes a day for the next five days, nothing could have saved London"; instead, "[the Luftwaffe\'s] morale in combat is definitely broken, and the RAF has been gaining in strength each week."<sup id="cite_ref-ingersoll1940_309-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ingersoll1940-309">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Both sides in the battle made exaggerated claims of numbers of enemy aircraft shot down. In general, claims were two to three times the actual numbers, because of the confusion of fighting in dynamic three-dimensional air battles. Postwar analysis of records has shown that between July and September, the RAF claimed 2,698 kills, while the Luftwaffe fighters claimed 3,198 RAF aircraft downed.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Total losses, and start and end dates for recorded losses, vary for both sides. Luftwaffe losses from 10 July to 30 October 1940 total 1,977 aircraft, including 243 twin- and 569 single-engined fighters, 822 bombers and 343 non combat types.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-23">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> In the same period, RAF Fighter Command aircraft losses number 1,087, including 53 twin-engined fighters.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> To the RAF figure should be added 376 Bomber Command and 148 Coastal Command aircraft lost conducting bombing, mining, and reconnaissance operations in defence of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_p._368_15-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_p._368-15">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is a consensus among historians that the Luftwaffe were unable to crush the RAF. <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Bungay" title="Stephen Bungay">Stephen Bungay</a> described Dowding and Park\'s strategy of choosing when to engage the enemy whilst maintaining a coherent force as vindicated; their leadership, and the subsequent debates about strategy and tactics, had created enmity among RAF senior commanders and both were sacked from their posts in the immediate aftermath of the battle.<sup id="cite_ref-310" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-310">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup> All things considered, the RAF proved to be a robust and capable organisation which was to use all the modern resources available to it to the maximum advantage.<sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup> Richard Evans wrote:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Irrespective of whether Hitler was really set on this course, he simply lacked the resources to establish the air superiority that was the sine qua non-of a successful crossing of the English Channel.  A third of the initial strength of the German air force, the Luftwaffe, had been lost in the western campaign in the spring.  The Germans lacked the trained pilots, the effective fighter aircraft, and the heavy bombers that would have been needed.<sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">&#91;nb 23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The Germans launched some spectacular attacks against important British industries, but they could not destroy the British industrial potential, and made little systematic effort to do so. Hindsight does not disguise the fact the threat to Fighter Command was very real, and for the participants it seemed as if there was a narrow margin between victory and defeat. Nevertheless, even if the German attacks on the 11 Group airfields which guarded southeast England and the approaches to London had continued, the RAF could have withdrawn to the Midlands out of German fighter range and continued the battle from there.<sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup> The victory was as much psychological as physical. Writes Alfred Price:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>The truth of the matter, borne out by the events of 18 August is more prosaic: neither by attacking the airfields, nor by attacking London, was the Luftwaffe likely to destroy Fighter Command. Given the size of the British fighter force and the general high quality of its equipment, training and morale, the Luftwaffe could have achieved no more than a Pyrrhic victory. During the action on 18 August it had cost the Luftwaffe five trained aircrew killed, wounded or taken prisoner, for each British fighter pilot killed or wounded; the ratio was similar on other days in the battle. And this ratio of 5:1 was very close to that between the number of German aircrew involved in the battle and those in Fighter Command. In other words the two sides were suffering almost the same losses in trained aircrew, in proportion to their overall strengths. In the Battle of Britain, for the first time during the Second World War, the German war machine had set itself a major task which it patently failed to achieve, and so demonstrated that it was not invincible. In stiffening the resolve of those determined to resist Hitler the battle was an important turning point in the conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>The British victory in the Battle of Britain was achieved at a heavy cost. Total British civilian losses from July to December 1940 were 23,002 dead and 32,138 wounded, with one of the largest single raids on 19 December 1940, in which almost 3,000 civilians died. With the culmination of the concentrated daylight raids, Britain was able to rebuild its military forces and establish itself as an Allied stronghold, later serving as a base from which <a href="/wiki/Operation_Overlord" title="Operation Overlord">the Liberation of Western Europe</a> was launched.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_2000,_p._388_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_2000,_p._388-33">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_Britain_Day">Battle of Britain Day</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Battle of Britain Day">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain_Day" title="Battle of Britain Day">Battle of Britain Day</a></div> \n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Never_was_so_much_owed_by_so_many_to_so_few.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Never_was_so_much_owed_by_so_many_to_so_few.jpg/170px-Never_was_so_much_owed_by_so_many_to_so_few.jpg" width="170" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Never_was_so_much_owed_by_so_many_to_so_few.jpg/255px-Never_was_so_much_owed_by_so_many_to_so_few.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Never_was_so_much_owed_by_so_many_to_so_few.jpg/340px-Never_was_so_much_owed_by_so_many_to_so_few.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="895" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Never_was_so_much_owed_by_so_many_to_so_few.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World War II poster containing the famous lines by Winston Churchill</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Britain_Anniversary,_1943_-_RAF_Parade_at_Buckingham_Palace_Art.IWMARTLD3911.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Battle_of_Britain_Anniversary%2C_1943_-_RAF_Parade_at_Buckingham_Palace_Art.IWMARTLD3911.jpg/220px-Battle_of_Britain_Anniversary%2C_1943_-_RAF_Parade_at_Buckingham_Palace_Art.IWMARTLD3911.jpg" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Battle_of_Britain_Anniversary%2C_1943_-_RAF_Parade_at_Buckingham_Palace_Art.IWMARTLD3911.jpg/330px-Battle_of_Britain_Anniversary%2C_1943_-_RAF_Parade_at_Buckingham_Palace_Art.IWMARTLD3911.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Battle_of_Britain_Anniversary%2C_1943_-_RAF_Parade_at_Buckingham_Palace_Art.IWMARTLD3911.jpg/440px-Battle_of_Britain_Anniversary%2C_1943_-_RAF_Parade_at_Buckingham_Palace_Art.IWMARTLD3911.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="504" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Britain_Anniversary,_1943_-_RAF_Parade_at_Buckingham_Palace_Art.IWMARTLD3911.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Battle of Britain anniversary parade at Buckingham Palace in 1943.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> summed up the effect of the battle and the contribution of RAF Fighter Command, RAF Bomber Command, RAF Coastal Command and the Fleet Air Arm with the words, "Never in the field of human conflict was <a href="/wiki/Never_was_so_much_owed_by_so_many_to_so_few" title="Never was so much owed by so many to so few">so much owed by so many to so few</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup> Pilots who fought in the battle have been known as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Few" title="The Few">The Few</a></i> ever since; at times being specially commemorated on 15 September, "<a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain_Day" title="Battle of Britain Day">Battle of Britain Day</a>". On this day in 1940, the Luftwaffe embarked on their largest bombing attack yet, forcing the engagement of the entirety of the RAF in defence of London and the South East, which resulted in a decisive British victory that proved to mark a turning point in Britain\'s favour.<sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Within the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a>, Battle of Britain Day has been observed more usually on the third Sunday in September, and even on the 2nd Thursday in September in some areas in the British <a href="/wiki/Channel_Islands" title="Channel Islands">Channel Islands</a>.\n</p><p>The day has been observed by many artists over the years, often with works that show the battle itself. Many mixed media artists have also created pieces in honour of the Battle of Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Memorials_and_museums">Memorials and museums</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Memorials and museums">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Plans for the Battle of Britain window in Westminster Abbey were begun during wartime, the committee chaired by Lords Trenchard and Dowding. Public donations paid for the window itself, this officially opened by King George VI on 10 July 1947. Although not actually an \'official\' memorial to the Battle of Britain in the sense that government paid for it, the window and chapel have since been viewed as such. During the late 1950s and 1960, various proposals were advanced for a national monument to the Battle of Britain, this also the focus of several letters in The Times. In 1960 the Conservative government decided against a further monument, taking the view that the credit should be shared more broadly than Fighter Command alone, and there was little public appetite for one. All subsequent memorials are the result of private subscription and initiative, as discussed below.<sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There are numerous memorials to the battle. The most important ones are the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain_Monument_in_London" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Britain Monument in London">Battle of Britain Monument in London</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain_Memorial,_Capel-le-Ferne" title="Battle of Britain Memorial, Capel-le-Ferne">Battle of Britain Memorial at Capel-le-Ferne</a> in Kent. <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> and <a href="/wiki/St_James%27s_Church,_Paddington" title="St James&#39;s Church, Paddington">St James\'s Church, Paddington</a> both have memorial windows to the battle, replacing windows that were destroyed during the campaign. There is also a memorial at the former <a href="/wiki/Croydon_Airport" title="Croydon Airport">Croydon Airport</a>, one of the RAF bases during the battle, and a memorial to the pilots at <a href="/wiki/Armadale_Castle" title="Armadale Castle">Armadale Castle</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Skye" title="Skye">Isle of Skye</a> in Scotland, which is topped by a raven sculpture.\n</p><p>There are also two museums to the battle: one at Hawkinge in <a href="/wiki/Kent_Battle_of_Britain_Museum" title="Kent Battle of Britain Museum">Kent</a> and one at <a href="/wiki/Stanmore" title="Stanmore">Stanmore</a> in London, at the former <a href="/wiki/RAF_Bentley_Priory" title="RAF Bentley Priory">RAF Bentley Priory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2015 the RAF created an online \'Battle of Britain 75th Anniversary Commemorative Mosaic\' composed of pictures of "the few" \xe2\x80\x93 the pilots and aircrew who fought in the battle \xe2\x80\x93 and "the many" \xe2\x80\x93 \'the often unsung others whose contribution during the Battle of Britain was also vital to the RAF\'s victory in the skies above Britain\', submitted by participants and their families.<sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">\n\t<li class="gallerycaption">Memorials to the Battle of Britain</li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 182.66666666667px"><div style="width: /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Monument of Polish Pilots, Northolt\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: In popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media_adaptations">Media adaptations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Media adaptations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The battle was the subject of the 1969 film <i><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain_(film)" title="Battle of Britain (film)">Battle of Britain</a></i>. The cast included <a href="/wiki/Laurence_Olivier" title="Laurence Olivier">Laurence Olivier</a> as <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Dowding" title="Hugh Dowding">Hugh Dowding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trevor_Howard" title="Trevor Howard">Trevor Howard</a> as <a href="/wiki/Keith_Park" title="Keith Park">Keith Park</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BOBFilm_324-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BOBFilm-324">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup> It also starred <a href="/wiki/Michael_Caine" title="Michael Caine">Michael Caine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Plummer" title="Christopher Plummer">Christopher Plummer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Shaw_(actor)" title="Robert Shaw (actor)">Robert Shaw</a> as Squadron Leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-BOBFilm_324-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BOBFilm-324">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup> Former participants of the battle served as technical advisors including <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Bader" title="Douglas Bader">Douglas Bader</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Galland" title="Adolf Galland">Adolf Galland</a> and Hugh Dowding. An Italian film around the same time entitled <i><a href="/wiki/Eagles_Over_London" title="Eagles Over London">Eagles Over London</a></i> (1969) also featured the Battle of Britain. The 1988 ITV mini-series <a href="/wiki/Piece_of_Cake_(TV_series)" title="Piece of Cake (TV series)"><i>Piece of Cake</i></a>, an aerial drama about a fictional Second World War RAF fighter squadron in 1940, features the battle. The <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech</a> film <i><a href="/wiki/Dark_Blue_World" title="Dark Blue World">Dark Blue World</a></i> (2001) also featured the battle, focusing on the Czech pilots who fought in the battle. A fictional version of the battle is shown in the 2001 movie, <i><a href="/wiki/Pearl_Harbor_(film)" title="Pearl Harbor (film)">Pearl Harbor</a></i>, in which the battle is depicted as still going on into 1941.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>It has also been the subject of many documentaries, including the 1941 <a href="/wiki/Allied_propaganda_film" class="mw-redirect" title="Allied propaganda film">Allied propaganda film</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Churchill%27s_Island" title="Churchill&#39;s Island">Churchill\'s Island</a></i>, winner of the first <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Documentary_Short_Subject" class="mw-redirect" title="Academy Award for Documentary Short Subject">Academy Award for Documentary Short Subject</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NFB_325-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NFB-325">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup>\nThere was also the 1943 <i><a href="/wiki/The_Battle_of_Britain" title="The Battle of Britain">The Battle of Britain</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Frank_Capra" title="Frank Capra">Frank Capra</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Why_We_Fight" title="Why We Fight">Why We Fight</a></i> series. It was included in an episode of 2004 BBC <a href="/wiki/Television_documentary" title="Television documentary">television documentary</a> series <i><a href="/wiki/Battlefield_Britain" title="Battlefield Britain">Battlefield Britain</a></i>.\nIn 2010, actor <a href="/wiki/Julian_Glover" title="Julian Glover">Julian Glover</a> played a 101-year-old Polish veteran RAF pilot in the short film <i>Battle for Britain</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-327" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-327">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_books">In books</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: In books">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><i>The Few</i>, a novel by <a href="/wiki/Alex_Kershaw" title="Alex Kershaw">Alex Kershaw</a>, tells the stories of the men who flew in the Battle of Britain. As of  2003<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, a Hollywood film similarly named <i>The Few</i> was in preparation for release in 2008, based on the story of real-life US pilot <a href="/wiki/Billy_Fiske" title="Billy Fiske">Billy Fiske</a>, who ignored his country\'s neutrality rules and volunteered for the RAF.  A <i><a href="/wiki/Variety_(magazine)" title="Variety (magazine)">Variety</a></i> magazine outline of the film\'s historical content<sup id="cite_ref-328" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-328">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup> was said in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Independent" title="The Independent">The Independent</a></i> to have been described by Bill Bond, who conceived the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain_Monument_in_London" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Britain Monument in London">Battle of Britain Monument in London</a>, as "Totally wrong. The whole bloody lot."<sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup> The project was later cancelled.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Miscellaneous">Miscellaneous</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" of the Southern Railway</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Polish_War_Memorial" title="Polish War Memorial">Polish War Memorial</a></li></ul></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Stacey, p.256: "Even as Britain braced itself to meet the attack of the Luftwaffe, the nice legalities of Commonwealth cooperation had to be observed. But the R.C.A.F., like the Canadian Army, was determined that there should be no possibility of these formalities conferring any advantage on the enemy. To avoid misunderstanding, delay and perhaps embarrassment, and doubtless to emphasize the point that No. 1 Squadron was, after all, a Canadian unit, Air Commodore Walsh early brought the necessity of conforming to the Visiting Forces Acts to the attention of the Air Officer Commanding No. 11 Group R.A.F., under whom the squadron would fight..."</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Polish, Czech and most other national contingents were, at this time, incorporated into the RAF itself: the Polish Air Force, for example, was not given sovereignty until June 1944.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Although under RAF operational control, RCAF pilots in the BoB were technically flying for the RCAF</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The British date the battle from 10 July to 31 October 1940, which represented the most intense period of daylight <a href="/wiki/Airstrike" title="Airstrike">bombing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Foreman_1989_8_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foreman_1989_8-4">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> German historians usually place the beginning of the battle in May 1940 and end it mid-August 1941, with the withdrawal of the <a href="/wiki/Bomber" title="Bomber">bomber</a> units in preparation for <a href="/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa" title="Operation Barbarossa">Operation <i>Barbarossa</i></a>, the campaign against the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, which began on 22 June 1941.<sup id="cite_ref-Foreman_1989_8_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foreman_1989_8-4">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example: Terraine states that the outcome was "decisive"; quoting <i>Luftwaffe</i> General <a href="/wiki/Werner_Kreipe" title="Werner Kreipe">Werner Kreipe</a>, who described it as a "strategic (Luftwaffe) failure" and "turning point in the Second World War". It also states the "German Air Force was bled almost to death, and suffered losses that could never be made good throughout the course of the war". Quoting Dr (Karl) Klee "The invasion and subjugation of Britain was made to depend on that battle, and its outcome therefore materially influenced the further course and fate of the war as a whole".<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><br />Other references that can be consulted include::Shulman 2004, p. 63.:Bungay 2000, p. 368.:Hough and Richards 2007, p. XV.:Overy 2001, p. 267.:Deighton 1980, p. 213.:Keegan 1997, p. 81.:Buell 2002, p. 83.:Terraine 1985, p. 181.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">754 single-seat fighters, 149 two-seat fighters, 560 bombers and 500 coastal aircraft. The RAF fighter strength given is for 0900 1 July 1940, while bomber strength is for 11 July 1940.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_p._107_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_p._107-10">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Figures taken from Quartermaster General 6th Battalion returns on 10 August 1940. According to these, the <i>Luftwaffe</i> deployed 3,358 aircraft against Britain, of which 2,550 were serviceable. The force was made up by 934 single-seat fighters, 289 two-seat fighters, 1,482 medium bombers, 327 dive-bombers, 195 reconnaissance and 93 coastal aircraft, including unserviceable aircraft. The number of serviceable aircraft amounted to 805 single-seat fighters, 224 two-seat fighters, 998 medium bombers, 261 dive-bombers, 151 reconnaissance and 80 coastal aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood_and_Dempster_2003,_p._318._12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood_and_Dempster_2003,_p._318.-12">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <i>Luftwaffe</i> possessed 4,074 aircraft, but not all of these were deployed against Britain. The force was made up of 1,107 single-seat fighters, 357 two-seat fighters, 1,380 medium bombers, 428 dive-bombers, 569 reconnaissance and 233 coastal aircraft, including unserviceable aircraft. The <i>Luftwaffe</i> air strength given is from the Quartermaster General 6th Battalion numbers for 29 June 1940.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_p._107_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_p._107-10">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">1,220 fighters (per type: 753 Hurricane, 467 Spitfire)<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> 376 bombers, 148 aircraft (RAF Coastal Command)<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_p._368_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_p._368-15">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> </span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The strategic bombing commenced after the Germans bombed London on 14 September 1940, followed by the RAF bombing of Berlin and of German air force bases in France. <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> withdrew his directive not to bomb population centres and ordered attacks on British cities.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-102"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-102">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bf 109E-3 and E-4s had this armament, while the E-1, which was still used in large numbers, was armed with four 7.92mm machine guns.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-120"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-120">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The inboard position of the upper wing roundels on the Spitfire strongly suggests this was a repainted captured Spitfire or a photo-reconnaissance model, at least one of which was captured in France.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-130"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-130">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The pilots occupying these administrative positions included such officers as Dowding, Park and Leigh-Mallory and the numbers actually fit to serve in front line fighter squadrons are open to question.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-133"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-133">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Polish units in the composition of the RAF taking part in the Battle of Britain In the Battle of Britain, first in composition, and then alongside the RAF fought four Polish squadrons: two bomber (300 and 301), 2 Hunting (302 and 303) and 81 Polish pilots in British squadrons, a total of 144 Polish pilots (killed 29 ), representing 5% of all the pilots of the RAF taking part in the battle. Poles shot down about 170 German aircraft, damaged 36, representing about 12% of the losses of the Luftwaffe. Squadron 303 was the best unit air, taking part in the Battle of Britain \xe2\x80\x93 reported shot down 126 Luftwaffe planes.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-152"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-152">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This was the turning radius of a Bf 109, meaning that both aircraft, if necessary, could turn together at high speed.<sup id="cite_ref-Bungay_2000,_p._259._132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bungay_2000,_p._259.-132">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-156"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-156">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This formation was developed based on principles formulated by the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">First World War</a> <a href="/wiki/Flying_ace" title="Flying ace">ace</a> <a href="/wiki/Oswald_Boelcke" title="Oswald Boelcke">Oswald Boelcke</a> in 1916. In 1934 the <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Air_Force" title="Finnish Air Force">Finnish Air Force</a> adopted similar formations, called <i>partio</i> (patrol; two aircraft) and <i>parvi</i> (two patrols; four aircraft),<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> for similar reasons, though Luftwaffe\' pilots during the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a> (led by <a href="/wiki/G%C3%BCnther_L%C3%BCtzow" title="G\xc3\xbcnther L\xc3\xbctzow">G\xc3\xbcnther L\xc3\xbctzow</a> and <a href="/wiki/Werner_M%C3%B6lders" title="Werner M\xc3\xb6lders">Werner M\xc3\xb6lders</a>, among others) are generally given credit.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-207"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-207">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This account is from <i>Warner 2005, p. 253</i> Another source, <i>Ramsay 1989, p. 555</i>, lists no aircrew casualties and three 109s in total destroyed or damaged.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-229"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-229">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Satellite" airfields were mostly fully equipped but did not have the sector control room which allowed "Sector" airfields such as Biggin Hill to monitor and control RAF fighter formations. RAF units from Sector airfields often flew into a satellite airfield for operations during the day, returning to their home airfield in the evenings.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-267"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-267">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Irving 1974, pp. 118\xe2\x80\x93119</i>: Irving\'s sources were General <a href="/wiki/Franz_Halder" title="Franz Halder">Franz Halder</a> and the OKW War Diary for 14 September 1940. <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Keitel" title="Wilhelm Keitel">Keitel</a>\'s notes, ND 803-PS, record the same.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-269"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-269">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bungay refers to the 14 September meeting with Milch and Jeschonnek. Hitler wanted to keep up the "moral" pressure on the British Government, in the hope it would crack. Bungay indicates that Hitler had changed his mind from the day before, refusing to call off the invasion for the time being.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-275"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-275">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Jeffrey Quill wrote of his combat experience whilst flying with No. 65 Squadron: <i>Nearly all our engagements with Me 109s took place at around 20,000 \xe2\x80\x93 25,000 ft. The Spitfire had the edge over them in speed and climb, and particularly in turning circle. (...) One engagement with several Me 109s at about 25,000 ft over the Channel sticks in my memory...I was now convinced that the Spitfire Mk I could readily out-turn the 109, certainly in the 20,000 ft region and probably at all heights.</i><sup id="cite_ref-spitfireperformance_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spitfireperformance-274">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-277"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-277">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bf 109 leaking valves, supercharger faults/failure.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-282"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-282">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This proposal has since been confused, or conflated, with a possible flight by HMG in exile.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-314"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-314">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The exact percentage was 28. The Luftwaffe deployed 5,638 aircraft for the campaign. 1,428 were destroyed and a further 488 were damaged, but were repairable.<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Britain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFPeszke1980">Peszke 1980</a>, p.&#160;134</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Foreman_1989_8-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Foreman_1989_8_4-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Foreman_1989_8_4-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFForeman1989">Foreman 1989</a>, p.&#160;8</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Hough, Ronald (26 July 2010). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.co.uk/books?id=57PgAwAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PR15&amp;dq=battle+of+britain+british+victory&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ved=0ahUKEwja1pnAwfLSAhWmLMAKHf4kAhUQ6AEIQDAH#v=onepage&amp;q=battle%20of%20britain%20british%20victory&amp;f=false">"<i>The Battle of Britain</i>"</a>.
